- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/187321/][Ext3 for large filesystems]: Mingming Cao's extents + 48-bit patch addresses 8TB limit from signed 32-bit block numbers; extent struct: ee_block (logical block), ee_len (count), ee_start_hi/ee_start (physical block, 48-bit total); enables 1024PB devices; extents overlay inode's 15-pointer array; indirect extent blocks for many extents; no dump/restore needed to upgrade existing filesystems; signed→unsigned block types throughout.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/187336/][Time for ext4?]: Debate on naming: Jeff Garzik wants ext4 for clean break since enabling extents sets incompatible feature flag making filesystem unreadable by older kernels; worry about ext3 code complexity with if(has_feature) conditionals; ext3 developers argue users shouldn't need backup/restore; Linus skeptical of major surgery but didn't rule it out; Andrew Morton suggested greenfield start maybe based on reiser4; eventual outcome: extents merged into ext3, later became ext4.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/186583/][How clean must the room be?]: acx100 wireless driver developed from reverse engineering binary-only TI driver; clean room technique requires separate doc-writer from coder to avoid code copying allegations; developers didn't separate these roles, leaving code "tainted" even if legally clean; some argue clean room is USA-only concept not legally required; SCO experience makes devs wary of merged code that might invite litigation; others say if developers certify no copying done (especially outside USA), should be merged; question of which country's laws should govern global OS kernel.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/186602/][The current state of the BusyBox project]: Rob Landley (new maintainer) describes BusyBox—single executable providing standard Linux utilities for embedded systems; replaces 21+ packages (coreutils, bzip2, diffutils, e2fsprogs, file, findutils, gawk, grep, inetutils, less, modutils, net-tools, procps, sed, shadow, sysklogd, sysvinit, tar, util-linux, vim); minimal cat.c is 40 lines vs GNU's 772; uClibc + Linux-Tiny + BusyBox can boot in 4MB RAM / 2MB disk; design goal is code size; new maintainer expanding to general purpose servers; future: standalone applet compilation, test suite, no-MMU support.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184673/][Virtualization: now what?]: Discussion of container/virtualization features needed for Linux: UTS namespace (system name info), PID virtualization (reusable IDs per container), SYSV IPC namespaces (semaphores, shared memory, message queues), time virtualization, user/group ID virtualization, network namespaces (per-container network interfaces/IPs); filesystem namespace already exists; most container concepts need all features; Savochkin suggested starting with hardest part (network namespaces) to detect if consensus achievable before investing too much effort.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184750/][A new generic IRQ layer]: Thomas Gleixner/Ingo Molnar's genirq patches unify IRQ handling across all architectures (ARM doesn't use current generic layer); new irq_chip structure with startup/shutdown/enable/disable/ack/end/mask/mask_ack/unmask/set_affinity/retrigger/set_type/set_wake methods; irq_desc gets handle_irq() method pointing to flow-specific handler (level-triggered, edge-triggered, simple, per-CPU); SA_TRIGGER_LOW/HIGH/FALLING/RISING flags for request_irq(); set_irq_type() changes flow type; set_irq_wake() controls wake-from-suspend; ARM main obstacle to merge.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184925/][Holes in the Linux random number generator?]: Gutterman/Pinkas/Reinman paper describes attack on Linux RNG forward security: compromising entropy pool lets attacker reverse RNG algorithm to recover previous states and old keys; Ted Ts'o: O(2^68) work to crack 1024-bit RSA key under specific conditions; attack requires already-rooted box; /dev/random blocking when low entropy is intentional design, not a bug; Alan Cox: better to refuse entropy than provide false entropy; /dev/urandom recommended for most uses.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184942/][How Sun's Java got into Debian]: Debian fast-tracked Sun Java into non-free under new DLJ license without usual debian-legal debate; DPL Anthony Towns cited Sun's public relations cooperation, estoppel argument from FAQ, and ability to drop if problems arise; controversy: whether non-free "is part of Debian", whether DLJ FAQ is legally binding, Sun's "in conjunction with" restriction on mixing with alternatives; process bypass criticized as undermining Debian's principles; Tom Marble argued DLJ allows shipping alternatives; Debian can simply remove Java if license enforcement fails.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184967/][Toward a free Java]: Mark Wielaard describes GNU Classpath/hacker cooperation since 2003; Red Hat Alternative Runtime Summit 2004 brought together GNU Classpath, Kaffe, GCJ, JamVM, IKVM/Mono; Eclipse packaging success story; Apache Harmony launched but plagued by miscommunication—IBM wanted proprietary J9 VM interface unsuitable for existing GNU Classpath users; only one Harmony dev showed at FOSDEM 2006; Apache TCK licensing concerns vs GPL+linking exception; GPLv3 will clarify system library exception and compatibility clauses; Sun now cooperating on combined testsuites.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/182874/][On the safety of Linux random numbers]: Matt Mackall removing SA_SAMPLE_RANDOM from network drivers; debate over whether network timing contributes meaningful entropy given attacker can observe/generate packets on same network segment; SHA-1 reversal to guess entropy pool requires O(2^68) work for 1024-bit RSA key; switch to dedicated hardware more practical; /dev/random blocks when low entropy, /dev/urandom continues with pseudo-random; network interrupt timing provides entropy but concerns about estimate accuracy; many systems lack other entropy sources so network timing unlikely to be removed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/183105/][The Xen patches]: 35-part Xen hypervisor patch series for mainline merge; creates new i386 subarchitecture for Xen guest kernel; changes: more interrupt vectors for pseudo-interrupts, event channel mechanism for hypervisor-guest communication, split initialization code for subarchitectures, dynamic tick support for idle VMs, separate timekeeping, virtual console/network/block devices; core kernel changes: synch_set_bit() always-atomic operations for SMP host with uniprocessor guests, apply_to_page_range() for PTE iteration; built-in ARP implementation defended for fast migration; /proc additions unpopular but being fixed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/183053/][Kernel bugs: out of control?]: Andrew Morton warned kernel is getting buggier; no good metrics—OSDL bug tracker underused, distributors don't share data; Dustin Kirkland's Google search normalized stats show stable/trending better for recent kernels; bugs not fixed because: developers prefer features over bugfixing, code is modular with unclear ownership, many subsystems lack active maintainers, hardware bugs hard to reproduce, users lack patience for debugging回合; solutions: full-time "bugmaster" position, developers branching outside their areas, formal regression tests (LTP underutilized), static analysis (sparse); Coverity scans found longstanding bugs; no quick fixes—kernel quality will take time to improve.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/182060/][Implementing network channels]: Van Jacobson's network channels concept for SMP-friendly high-speed networking; Kelly Daly/Rusty Russell posted channel_ring struct (256 pages mapped via mmap to user space); producer vars at ring start, consumer vars at end to avoid cache line bouncing; user creates PF_VJCHAN socket, mmap buffer, poll/select for data, increment index to free buffers; driver uses vj_get_buffer()/vj_netif_rx(); contemporary smart network cards can choose correct buffer before DMA; issues: David Miller dislikes preallocated buffers, netfilter bypass (zero-copy shorts filtering on established connections), whether to move TCP to user-space entirely; current implementations are learning exercises, not final design.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/182080/][Xen and the new processors]: Intel VT-x (VMX root/non-root modes) and AMD SVM (Host/Guest modes) provide hardware virtualization allowing unmodified guest OSes; Intel VMCS and AMD VMCB structures handle virtualization management with exit reasons; AMD SVM has tagged TLB using ASID to avoid flushes on host/guest context switches; Intel VT-d and AMD IOMMU enable direct I/O device assignment to VMs with DMA remapping; common HVM API abstracts VT-x and SVM differences; both use QEMU for I/O emulation (performance cost); VT-x requires hvmloader with VM86 mode for guest boot since VT-x guests can't start in real mode; SVM supports real mode directly; Xen unstable repo supports both since Jan 2006.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/181508/][The AppArmor debate begins]: Novell's AppArmor LSM submitted for mainline merge; criticism: pathname-based policies vs SELinux label-based approach; problem: restricting /etc/shadow doesn't prevent access via hardlinks or alternate paths to same file; AppArmor uses learning mode (logs app behavior, generates profile) unlike SELinux's manual policy; AppArmor claims simpler for "mere mortals" vs SELinux complexity; LSM API poorly suited to pathname policies requiring expensive pathname translation; proponents argue AppArmor only aims to limit compromised apps, not solve all security; technical soundness questioned by SELinux developers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/180387/][write(), thread safety, and POSIX]: Dan Bonachea found write() not thread-safe—concurrent writes to same fd can lose data as threads get same file position; current implementation reads pos without locking; POSIX requires write() thread-safety; solutions: lock entire write() (too expensive, prevents simultaneous writes), O_APPEND (atomic), userspace locking; Linus proposed f_pos_lock mutex in struct file to serialize position access only, keeping async I/O and pwrite() fast; eventually merged in Linux 3.14 (2014).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/180633/][Some notes on Linux and free drivers]: ZDNet coverage of proprietary driver debate; Nvidia claims graphics driver "too hard" for open source developers and customers don't ask; rebuttal: Linux supports everything from embedded to supercomputers, graphics drivers aren't uniquely difficult; ATI mentions kernel API fluidity burden; author's argument: mainline kernel is solution—gets drivers working across all distributions and architectures, long-term support when vendor abandons driver; call to pressure system integrators to demand free drivers; hardware vendors should release specs and let community write drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/179597/][How Mono got into Fedora]: Red Hat reversed policy allowing Mono into Fedora due to Microsoft .NET patent concerns; Open Invention Network (OIN) created to acquire patents for free software defense; OIN policy: royalty-free patents to companies agreeing not to assert against Linux and related apps; Mono on protected list—attackers risk OIN countersuit; Novell bought Commerce One XML web services patents ($15.5M), contributed to OIN; patents considered strong enough to protect Mono; criticism: only works against direct litigants, not patent trolls; doesn't protect non-Linux systems; OIN is incremental step, not complete patent solution.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/179361/][Containers and lightweight virtualization]: Container-based virtualization (Linux-VServer, OpenVZ, Virtuozzo) vs full virtualization (Xen, UML); containers share same kernel, lose ability to run different OSes but have better performance; over 300,000 OpenVZ virtual environments; developers unifying approach—joint patch development then forwarding to mainline; first patch: UTS namespace virtualization (per-container hostname/domainname); future: PID virtualization, SYSV IPC, network namespaces; SELinux can use single policy with containers via type hierarchy; memory footprint concerns for embedded use.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/178253/][Priority inheritance in the kernel]: Priority inversion: low-priority process holds lock, high-priority waits, medium-priority preempts low-priority indefinitely; Mars Pathfinder experienced this; priority inheritance boosts lock holder to highest waiter's priority; Ingo Molnar's PI-futex: FUTEX_LOCK_PI/UNLOCK_PI for user-space mutexes with priority inheritance; rt_mutex new primitive; LINUX hosts blocked on lock boost priority through chain; Linus initially opposed ("Friends don't let friends use priority inheritance") but Ingo's careful implementation may gain acceptance; part of -RT realtime patchset.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/178634/][On the safety of the sysfs interfaces]: CVE-2006-1055 fix: sysfs attribute writes limited to PAGE_SIZE-1 bytes (4095) and null-terminated before passing to store(); many store() implementations assume null-terminated input without checking length; Al Viro批评 even reporter's own code used sscanf("%x") on 4096 spaces which overflows; "clear majority of sysfs-related code in drivers is junk"—interface misdesigned causing widespread misuse; no good way to verify interface correctness.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/177118/][A framework for page replacement policies]: Peter Zijlstra's 34-part patch creates API for pluggable page replacement algorithms (selectable at kernel config); four implementations included: modified LRU (current), CLOCK-PRO, CART technique, random; API functions: page_replace_init(), page_replace_candidates(), page_replace_hint_active/use_once(); Andrew Morton "Holy cow" reaction; Linus called it "university research" preferring incremental fixes over wholesale changes; similar to pluggable schedulers that never merged.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/176911/][The new pselect() system call]: pselect()/ppoll() added in 2.6.16 solve race between signal delivery and select/poll; traditional self-pipe trick has race window; pselect() atomically sets signal mask then calls select(); glibc 2.4 wrapper handles underlying syscall details (sigset_t size args, timeout copy); ppoll() adds same to poll(); epoll_pwait coming; timeout uses struct timespec for nanosecond precision.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/177037/][SQL injection attacks]: Jake Edge explains SQL injection: ' OR 1=1 authentication bypass, UNION attacks to extract passwords, DROP TABLE, semicolon-separated multiple queries (PostgreSQL); search functions especially dangerous displaying output; MS-SQL provides schema to all tables—easiest to attack; addslashes() circumventable via CHAR() injection, hex encoding; defenses: prepared statements with placeholders (best), db_quote() per-database functions, stored procedures running with limited privileges.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/174494/][Double kfree() errors]: Coverity scan found double-free bugs; Dave Jones proposed poisoning pointers after kfree() but fails: NULL must remain legal to free twice, pointer isn't always assignable lvalue; slab poisoning (0x5a uninit, 0x6b freed) already exists in CONFIG_DEBUG_SLAB but slows system 40-70%—rarely used; solution: require developers use slab poisoning in testing, Coverity for static analysis coverage.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/174641/][RCU and open file accounting]: David Miller hit file-max limit on 32-cpu Niagara 16GB doing make -j8192; problem: file count decremented in slab destructor (delayed), RCU callback queue grows huge with many open/close causing SLAB fragmentation and OOM; 2.6.14 limited callbacks per iteration but queue still grows; Dipankar Sarma's patch: configurable batch limit, high-water mark triggers forced reschedule on all CPUs to clear queue; fixed Niagara problem.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/173740/][ABI stability documentation]: Greg Kroah-Hartman's 5-tier ABI stability classification: Stable (2+ years, syscall interface), Testing (/sys/class), Unstable (/sys/devices), Private (ALSA—behind library), Obsolete (devfs); Linus dislikes "unstable" as "excuses for bad habits" but acknowledged interfaces need stress-testing; documentation helps set user expectations about what can change.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/169961/][Van Jacobson's network channels]: Van Jacobson's linux.conf.au talk on channel-based networking to fix SMP scalability; problem: memory latency 50ns vs CPU 1ns—cache behavior dominates; current stack has poor cache behavior across interrupt/softirq/socket layers, locking, linked-list queues; solution: circular buffer "channels" requiring no locks, no shared writable cache lines; 3-stage implementation: driver channel (58% CPU), socket channel (28% CPU), application channel (14% CPU); 2x bandwidth improvement; e1000 driver shrunk from 700 to 300 lines; LD_PRELOAD library approach for user-space TCP.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/170003/][The search for fast, scalable counters]: atomic_t causes cache line bouncing on SMP—expensive locked operations; percpu_counter uses per-CPU local counts, fast but approximate (underestimates on growing counters); local_t with two per-CPU counters + flag for exact reads when needed via spinlock; 5% Apache benchmark improvement changing one atomic_t to percpu_counter; local_t alternative more complex but exact on demand.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/168894/][Review: Understanding Linux Network Internals]]: O'Reilly book by Christian Benvenuti (1000+ pages); covers network device drivers, packet tx/rx, IPv4, ICMP, ARP/neighbor code, routing, bridging (110 pages); Complaints: kernel version not disclosed, some sections outdated (interrupt handler prototype from 2.5.68), missing driver tx/rx function guidance; missing TCP, IPv6, IPsec, netfilter, traffic control; reviewer would trade bridging chapters for TCP coverage; welcome addition but incomplete.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/168094/][Using open-source tools for documenting research]]: Carl Bolduc's guide for scientists transitioning to Linux; OpenOffice.org for Word compatibility and PDF export; LaTeX/BibTeX for typesetting and bibliography (de facto standard in hard sciences); LyX GUI front-end; gBib/JabRef for reference management; Gnuplot for 2D/3D plotting; Inkscape for vector graphics; Gimp for image processing; Drawbacks: learning curve, coworker .doc compatibility; Advantages: free, robust, virus-free, applications share data via EPS/LaTeX formats.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/167897/][The high-resolution timer API]: Thomas Gleixner's hrtimers merged into 2.6.16; ktime_t stores nanoseconds (64-bit integer on 64-bit, two 32-bit fields [sec,nsec] on 32-bit for efficiency); CLOCK_MONOTONIC (always forward) vs CLOCK_REALTIME (wall clock); hrtimer_init() binds timer to clock; hrtimer_start(mode=HRTIMER_ABS/REL); callback returns HRTIMER_NORESTART or HRTIMER_RESTART; hrtimer_forward() advances by interval for recurring timers; hrtimer_cancel() ensures timer inactive; still works at HZ resolution until high-res clock patches merge.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/168093/][Containers and PID virtualization]: IBM container patches for checkpoint/restart/migration; /proc/container creates containers; each container has separate PID space; upper 9 bits of 32-bit PID hold pidspace number; real vs virtual PIDs throughout kernel (current->pid→tsk_pid/tsk_vpid); Alan Cox opposed: "obscure, weird...eliminated by Xen"; developers argue checkpoint/restart valuable for virtualization; alternative: replace in-kernel PID usage with task pointers to reduce virtualization overhead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/167825/][GPLv3: a first look]: FSF GPLv3 draft analysis; DRM provisions: anti-DRM declarations, GPL code can't be part of TPM, must provide decryption keys; patent retaliation: suing over patents = lose right to use that software; implicit patent license grant overrides field-of-use restrictions; termination: copyright holder must actively terminate vs automatic; addresses "web services loophole" (optional requirement to provide source); compatible with more licenses; expected adoption Jan 2007.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/168248/][The .NET API patent, mono, and GNOME]: Microsoft's .NET API patent application nearing abandonment—3-month reply deadline passed without response; if granted would devastate Mono since API forms core interface; Fedora Foundation decided to include Mono (resolved "business considerations"); Red Hat's counsel cites patent pool protections; GNOME debate since 2004 on using Mono for core apps (Havoc Pennington's essay); several Mono apps now exist but unclear patent status stunted contributions; alternatives like OCaml Gtk bindings (lablgtk2) and Haskell Gtk2Hs exist.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/167034/][The mutex API]: Mutex merged into 2.6.16 after only one -mm release; struct mutex with atomic_t count (1=available, 0=locked, negative=waiting), spinlock_t wait_lock, list_head wait_list; API: DEFINE_MUTEX(), mutex_init(), mutex_lock()/mutex_lock_interruptible()/mutex_trylock(), mutex_unlock(), mutex_is_locked(); unlock forbidden from interrupt context; semaphores officially deprecated—new code must use mutexes; rwsem unaffected; counting semaphore users need new architecture-independent type before migration path exists.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/167270/][Linux and wireless networking]: Jeff Garzik: "another banner year for Linux's superior crappy wireless support"; 802.11 stack lacks softmac support for adapters doing MAC in firmware; DeviceScape stack proposed but networking maintainers resist replacement—prefer fixing current stack; wireless extensions ioctl() deprecated, netlink-based interface preferred over sysfs (atomic multi-parameter updates difficult with one-value-per-file); John Linville new wireless maintainer; wireless summit planned April in Portland.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/164121/][SMP alternatives]: Gerd Knorr's SMP alternatives patch expands kernel's alternative() patching mechanism; alternative_smp() macro selects UP vs SMP implementations at boot; main use: patching spinlocks to no-ops on uniprocessor, removing lock prefixes; preserves lock state counters for hotplug scenarios; enables CPU hotplug to dynamically switch between SMP/UP kernel mode at runtime—useful for Xen virtual CPUs that can be added/removed on the fly without reboot; self-modifying kernel code for virtualization.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/162966/][Memory copies in hardware]: Intel I/OAT (I/O acceleration technology) asynchronous DMA engine API for offloading memory copies; dma_async_client_register/unregister for DMA clients, dma_async_client_chan_request for channels (request one per CPU), dma_async_memcpy_buf_to_buf/pg_to_pg for async copies, dma_async_memcpy_issue_pending() starts operation, dma_async_wait_for_completion() waits via busy-loop; concerns: cache coherency issues (DMA to cached memory), whether hardware copy overhead worthwhile for small operations, future transforms support wanted; hardware due early 2006.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/162941/][The Boost C++ Libraries]: Matthew Vogt's review of Boost 1.33.1 (63 libraries); under Boost Software License (MIT-like, allows relicensing object code); highlights: threads, regex, filesystem, smart_ptr, function/bind, signals, unit testing; peer-review process ensures quality; 10 of 14 TR1 C++ standard proposals came from Boost; libraries designed to complement Standard Library; Lambda functions reviewed as clever but complex workaround for missing core language feature; conclusion: best C++ library resource available.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/161190/][Understanding the Linux Kernel, 3rd Edition]: Book review of UTLK 3rd ed (Bovet/Cesati, 900pp); covers 2.6.11 kernel; i386-focused with segmentation/GDT details; step-by-step kernel function analysis with flowcharts; "how it works" study guide not "how to hack" manual; topics: memory management, process scheduling, signals, VFS, timing, I/O; omissions: networking, security, device drivers, other archs; minor errors (semaphore sleep claim); useful reference with kernel function index; recommended for L1 bookshelf.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/162209/][The status of the GNU Fortran project]: Kenneth Geisshirt's overview of free Fortran compilers; GNU Fortran 77 (g77) discontinued after满足用户需求; G95 project implements Fortran 95, claims no known bugs; GCC 4.0 includes fully-compliant Fortran 95 frontend (gfortran) using Tree-SSA; intrinsic functions implemented but limited I/O intrinsics; can resume from core dumps (QUIT signal); documentation not well maintained; many scientific packages compile with gfortran.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/161106/][Open document formats and the path to world domination]: Glyn Moody's analysis of ODF vs Microsoft's "open standardization" of Office formats; Massachusetts adopted ODF as official format causing Microsoft reaction; EU backed ODF through ISO standardization; Sun (StarOffice) and IBM (Workplace) support ODF; Microsoft announced "open" standardization to preempt ISO approval; ODF adoption could break Microsoft's file format lock-in similar to WordPerfect/ Lotus decline; ODF plugin for Office, accessibility improvements, ODF reader, and lightweight editor needed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/160908/][The end of USENET]: Jon Cort's obituary for USENET as mainstream medium; Rogers and AOL dropped netnews; Globe and Mail declared death; USENET survived earlier scares but now too spam-prone, easy to forge control messages; comp.*/sci.* hierarchies still useful with little spam; Google Groups preserves archives; alternatives like mailing lists/web forums inferior for technical discussion; signal-to-noise ratio sunk; commercial providers pulling plug due to bandwidth cost vs benefit.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/160953/][Making notifiers safe]: Chandra Seetharaman's patch fixes race conditions in kernel notifier chains; original notifier_call_chain() had no locking while calling notifiers; spinlock can't work (some notifiers sleep); solution: split into ATOMIC_NOTIFIER and BLOCKING_NOTIFIER types; BLOCKING uses rwsem semaphore for safe traversal; ATOMIC uses rcu_read_lock(); unregister uses synchronize_rcu() before freeing notifier block; notifiers cannot remove themselves (would deadlock); new notifier_head struct and NOTIFIER_HEAD macro.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/159313/][On binary drivers and stable interfaces]: Editorial explaining kernel developers' opposition to closed-source drivers: vendor dependency, short product lifecycles, no community fixes, API changes break binaries, kernel devs can't debug proprietary code, limited architecture support, potential GPL copyright issues; Linus 1999 quote: binary module users are on their own; solution: use hardware with free drivers; recalcitrant vendors will eventually come around as Linux popularity grows.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/157151/][Another approach to page table scalability]: Hugh Dickins' finer-grained page table locking; replaces single address space page table lock with per-page-table-page locks; eliminates contention on large SMP systems especially for multi-threaded processes; enables concurrent page reclaim in one address space region while faulting in another; stores lock in struct page private field (unused on page table pages) via preprocessor trickery; only enabled on 4+ CPU kernels to avoid overhead on smaller systems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/152580/][The relevance of the Linux Standard Base]: LSB created 1998 to prevent Unix-style fragmentation; 7 years later LSB 3.0 requires gcc-3.4+, has graphics/core variants, 8 architectures; greeted with yawn—few apps target LSB, vendors certify specific distros instead; Ulrich Drepper claims LSB lacks value; positive effects: brought distributions closer, raised divergence awareness; Linux avoided fragmentation due to common kernel/app base and free licensing; LSB helps codify expectations and keep Linux coherent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/188056/][KHB: Transparent support for large pages]: Valerie Aurora reviews "Practical, Transparent Operating System Support for Superpages" (Navarro et al., 2002) on FreeBSD/Alpha; reservation-based design reserves pages on fault, promotes to large page when fully populated; demotion when pages unwritten or under memory pressure; uses LRU-based preemptable reservations; radix tree population map tracks allocations; page replacement daemon runs on low contiguity not just low memory; pinned pages allocated in clusters to avoid fragmentation; 3500 lines of code; 33/35 apps improved, worst case 2-9% degradation; Linux HugeTLB is static/non-transparent, requires explicit allocation; Gelato@UNSW working on Itanium dynamic superpages; transparent large pages need VM redesign.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/187979/][Detecting kernel memory leaks]: Catalin Marinas' kmemleak detector using radix tree instrumentation of slab allocator; scan-and-mark algorithm similar to garbage collection; white list = potential leaks, gray list = reachable memory; records stack traces for allocations; handles interior pointers via container_of() macro tracking aliases; special handling for vmalloc, annotations suppress false positives; read via /sys/kernel/debug/memleak; found real ACPI memory leaks; Ingo Molnar proposed retaining type information for faster scanning and pointer type validation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/188371/][Why the KDE project switched to CMake]: KDE moved from autotools ("auto-hell") to CMake for KDE4; 1200+ developers, 4M lines code, 300+ commits/day; initially tried SCons/bksys but abandoned due to immature config system, poor non-Linux platform support, no upstream SCons developer engagement; CMake chosen—supports FIND_PATH/LIBRARY/PROGRAM, CHECK_FUNCTION_EXISTS/HEADER_EXISTS for platform introspection, builds shared libs cross-platform; clean syntax, generates MSVC projects plus Unix makefiles.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/175432/][Trees I: Radix trees]: Linux radix tree associates pointer values with integer keys; 64 slots per node (6 bits indexed); sparse storage efficient; used by address_space for page tracking (dirty/writeback pages), PowerPC IRQ mapping, NFS inode request tracking; API: RADIX_TREE(), radix_tree_insert/delete/lookup, radix_tree_preload for guaranteed allocation, radix_tree_gang_lookup for batch operations; tags system for marking entries (dirty/writeback); no internal locking; Nick Piggin's RCU patch for lockless lookups; no destroy function—trees assumed permanent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/184495/][Trees II: red-black trees]: Linux rbtrees are semi-balanced binary trees with red/black coloring rules ensuring approximate balance; O(log n) lookups, fast bounded insert/delete; used by I/O schedulers (anticipatory/deadline/CFQ), high-res timers, ext3 directories, VMAs, epoll fds, crypto keys, HTB network scheduler; requires embedding struct rb_node in your struct; no comparison callback—write search/insert yourself using rb_link_node/rb_insert_color; container_of via rb_entry(); no internal locking; rb_erase for removal, rb_replace_node for replacement.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/188059/][KHB: Failure-oblivious computing]: Valerie Aurora reviews Rinard's failure-oblivious computing (OSDI 2004); detects memory errors via safe-C compiler, silently discards out-of-bounds writes, returns manufactured values (0,1,2,0,1,3...) for out-of-bounds reads; transforms fatal errors into benign bugs; works for short error-propagation applications (web servers, mail servers); transforms memory errors into input errors program already handles; tested on Sendmail, Pine, Apache, Midnight Commander—failure-oblivious versions performed acceptably, no new bugs; Pine buffer overflow became feature when From field truncated silently; Apache performed better than safe-C version (avoided thread death overhead); not suitable for scientific computing where wrong value corrupts results; risks: may hide bugs from developers, potential new security holes if clipped data affects security decisions.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/191004/][initramfs and where user space truly begins]: H. Peter Anvin's klibc patches enable moving boot code from kernel to user-space; klibc provides minimal C library for early boot, kinit handles root mounting (ext2/3, NFS, RAID, LVM, crypto, DHCP/BOOTP); questions whether klibc/kinit should be part of kernel tree or maintained separately like other user-space code; Ted Ts'o argues kinit SHOULD be merged to ensure kernel/initramfs compatibility across distributions; concern about distribution-specific init code breaking vanilla kernel boots.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/190966/][execns()]: Cedric Le Goater's patch adds user namespace for container isolation; find_user() global user_struct enforces per-UID resource limits across system; proposed solution: execns() syscall combines namespace unsharing with exec(); closes all files, starts program with clean namespace; avoids complexity of fixing unshare() to handle dangling references to SYSV IPC/semaphores.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/191059/][Crash-only software: More than meets the eye]: Valerie Aurora reviews Candea/Fox crash-only software paper (HotOS 2003); crash recovery often faster than clean shutdown (RedHat 8 ext3: 75s vs 104s, Windows XP: 48s vs 61s); crash-only = crashes safely and recovers quickly, no clean shutdown; misconceptions: not lazy programming, crash mechanism must be external, checkpoint frequency tradeoff (not too often/not at all), state is volatile vs persistent; robust futex helps with crash-only components; chunkfs applies crash-only design to file systems for individual chunk recovery.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/191119/][OpenDocument: cleared for use?]: SFLC legal opinion cleared OpenDocument Format for free software use but scope limited—only surveyed OASIS Technical Committee members' patent disclosures; Sun's reasonable terms; didn't search all possible patents; disclaimer: non-committee members may assert patents, programs with additional functionality may practice licensed claims outside field-of-use restriction; conclusion: ODF not fully "cleared" but one patent problem category removed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/190847/][The end of the multiarch era?]: Fedora x86-64 now has native 64-bit OpenOffice—editor's system no longer needs 32-bit packages; yum had file conflict between i386 and x86_64 OpenOffice versions during update; multiarch support useful for migrations and proprietary apps but causes administrative nightmares with duplicate libraries; /lib64 not the answer; Debian's multiarch approach better but complex.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/191900/][OLS: Open source graphics drivers]: David Airlie's talk: Intel chipsets best supported (company is friendly); ATI "former leading light" now uncooperative—R200 free driver faster than binary fglrx; R300/R400 reverse engineering progressing, R500 impossible without cooperation; Nvidia has X.org 2D driver ("written in hex") plus binary 3D; Nouveau project reverse-engineering Nvidia 3D; notes ATI/Nvidia withdrew support around same time they got Xbox contracts.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/193183/][GPLv3 beta 2 and LGPLv3 beta 1]: FSF second draft changes: anti-DRM provision tweaked but intent unchanged (must provide keys to run modified software); patent term changed from explicit license grant to covenant not to assert; removed prohibition on distribution to invade privacy; removed 10x cost limit for source distribution (back to reasonable cost); free download for 3 years satisfies source requirement; added prohibition on additional restrictions (choice-of-venue, arbitration, attorney's fees); LGPLv3 much shorter as patch to GPLv3.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/193851/][ATI, AMD, and free drivers]: Stacey Quandt's analysis of AMD-ATI merger implications for open source; AMD has strong Linux community relationships unlike Intel's competitor; potential for open source drivers as tactical outcome of larger strategy; Intel provides free Linux graphics drivers; merger expected close end 2006, changes likely in early 2007.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/193663/][Debating reiser4 - again]: Hans Reiser pushing for 2.6.19 inclusion after ~4 years; "files as directories" feature removed due to locking problems; plugins controversial but cannot be loaded at runtime (no copyright issues); Andrew Morton says plugins are just internal abstraction layer, not real plugins; outstanding problems: no direct I/O (almost done), no extended attributes (blocks SELinux support); may finally get merged despite remaining limitations.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/194543/][Connecting Linux to hypervisors]: paravirt_ops vs VMI for Linux kernel hypervisor connection; VMI uses fixed binary ABI with hypervisor ROM loaded at boot—controversial as binary blob; paravirt_ops is open source structure with function pointers that can change like other kernel APIs; includes self-patching for performance; developed by Xen and VMWare; target 2.6.19 merge.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/194701/][A couple of lessons on the hazards of proprietary software]: Hoboken NJ robotic parking garage held hostage by Robotic Parking Systems after contract dispute—cars trapped inside; city paying $5500/month to license software; Dr. Notes medical records software cut off Georgia medical practice when they refused 4x support fee increase; lesson: proprietary software gives vendor control over critical business data.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/195828/][Letters to the editor]]: Reader letter from Karol Lewandowski arguing free documentation matters more than free drivers; NDA-written r200 driver still dependent on small team; Intel "free drivers" lack documentation; Broadcom wifi in mainline while Ralink not—preference should go to companies providing free docs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/196444/][Old kernels and new compilers]: Willy Tarreau maintaining 2.4 kernel considering gcc 4.x compatibility patches; administrators building 2.4 on modern desktops can't find old compilers; Ultra-stable site users prefer 2.4 for known reliability track record; Adrian Bunk maintaining long-term 2.6.16 as future stable base; 2.6 development model makes multi-year stability harder to guarantee.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/196721/][Kevents and review of new APIs]: Kevents unified event API momentum for 2.6.19; mmap shared memory ring buffer for fast event consumption without syscalls; current implementation requires app to still call kernel since no way to notify consumed events; concerns about API documentation requirements for user-space interfaces; comparison to FreeBSD kqueue—Andrew Morton suggests copying kqueue exactly if no problems identified.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/196292/][KHB: A Filesystems reading list]: Valerie Aurora's recommended file system papers: FFS (McKusick et al) for UNIX file system basics, Vnodes/VFS (Sun) for multi-filesystem abstraction, NFS (Sandberg et al), journaling (Tweedie's ext3 paper), log-structured file systems (Rosenblum/Ousterhout—no production use today), soft updates (McKusick/Ganger—complex, BSD only), WAFL (NetApp copy-on-write), XFS scalability paper, "Utility of File Names" (predicting file behavior from names). Notable authors: Bill Joy, Larry McVoy, Mendel Rosenblum, John Ousterhout—all became founders of successful companies.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/196641/][Resolved: firmware is not software]: Debian debate on firmware inetch; some developers want all firmware source required under DFSG; proposed resolution: firmware is "data" not "software" under DFSG source requirements; Sven Luther opposed—calling firmware "data" is dishonest and leads to non-free distro; alternative: temporarily waive DFSG requirement for firmware blobs; issue deferred again.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/196523/][Who maintains RPM?]: RPM maintainer Jeff Johnson left Red Hat but still maintains RPM; bug fix denial debates dragged on for years; distributions stuck on RPM 4.4.2 while upstream is 4.4.5+; Fedora/Red Hat effectively forked RPM; Paul Nasrat doing work at Red Hat but no clear upstream ownership; needs formal acknowledgment of fork or return to single upstream.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/197433/][Resource beancounters]: OpenVZ's beancounter system for container resource tracking; bean_counter struct tracks held resources (currently kernel memory only) with barrier/limit pairs; __GFP_BC flags charge allocations to current beancounter; barrier limit fails allocations but page table faults proceed to "limit" for safety; shared pages charged fractionally (1/2 if 2 users); used in production for years; alternative to CKRM/resource groups.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/197307/][The Linux Standard Base gets some applications]: First LSB-certified applications: RealPlayer and MySQL—both already worked on Linux before certification; LSB launched 1998 with goal of commercial app portability; only 2 apps certified in 8 years; commercial apps (EDA, SAP) still target Red Hat/SUSE only, not LSB; question remains whether any new Linux-native apps will emerge with LSB certification.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/198202/][Support for drivers in user space]: Thomas Gleixner's IIO (industrial I/O) module enables user-space PCI drivers with small kernel stub; iio_device struct registers physical address, interrupt handler; user-space driver does bulk of work, kernel stub handles interrupt acknowledgment then notifies user-space via event device node or SIGIO; could ATI/Nvidia use this? Concerns: GPL implications of mixing kernel stub with proprietary user-space driver; suspend/resume more difficult.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/199200/][A survey of the DocBook landscape]]: DocBook is an OASIS-developed markup language for software documentation semantics, used by KDE, GNOME, and LDP. DocBook 4.x (current stable) uses DTDs for validation; DocBook 5 (coming soon at time of writing) switches to RELAX NG schemas, adds XML namespace support, uses XLink for hyperlinks, and XInclude for transclusion. DocBook 5 breaks SGML compatibility to better integrate with XML technologies while maintaining DocBook 4 authoring patterns.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/198988/][Memory-mapped I/O barriers]]: Discusses a bug in the tg3 Ethernet driver where MMIO writes could be reordered with preceding regular memory writes, causing the device to see stale data. Various proposals emerged: adding explicit barriers like mem_to_io_barrier(), redefining writel()/readX() to include barriers, creating spin_lock_io()/spin_unlock_io() variants, or providing ordered (__writel) and unordered (__raw_writel) MMIO accessors. Linus preferred explicit barriers over changing MMIO semantics due to performance costs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/199004/][A bid to resurrect Linux capabilities]]: David Madore's patches to fix Linux's broken capabilities implementation. Expands capabilities from 32 to 64 bits, implements correct capability inheritance (combining process and file masks), and introduces "regular capabilities" (fork, exec, file access) that processes have by default and can drop for security. Ted Tso objected to the backward-compatible behavior for files without capability masks. Some argued SELinux provides similar functionality with labels, though capability masks avoid filesystem relabeling.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/199343/][KHB: Dynamic Instrumentation of Production Systems (DTrace)]]: Book review of the DTrace paper (Solaris tracing system). DTrace provides safe, zero-probe-effect tracing for production systems using providers (kernel modules creating probes), predicates (filtering), aggregations (in-kernel data coalescing), and speculative tracing (conditional data retention). Its D scripting language runs in a safe in-kernel DIF emulator. Linux equivalents are SystemTap (higher-level) and KProbes (low-level infrastructure); SystemTap was not yet in mainline at time of writing.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/200075/][Perl 6? Yeah, right.]]: Overview of Perl 6 design featuring coroutines, lazy evaluation, junctions, hyperoperators, and "Rules" (regexes with LL/LR grammar support). Perl 6 is defined by its official test suite rather than a single implementation. Pugs (Perl 6 in Haskell) and Parrot (multi-language VM from Python/Perl merger April Fools joke) enable running Perl 6 code today. The language remains backward-compatible in spirit but syntactically overhauled.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/200059/][Tracing infrastructures]]: LTT (Linux Trace Toolkit) vs KProbes debate over static vs dynamic tracing. Static tracepoints (LTT) are faster when enabled but add small overhead when disabled; dynamic tracepoints (KProbes) have ~10x higher CPU cost when active but zero overhead when unused. Debates also covered maintenance burden, local variable access, and architecture support. Consensus: use "markers" (placeholder locations) rather than hardcoded tracepoints to allow dynamic insertion later.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/200073/][Another container implementation]]: Rohit Seth's container patches via configfs interface for aggregate resource control. Containers support page_limit limits and runtime addtask/rmtask of processes. Lacks namespace isolation (processes see entire system) and differs from beancounters/OpenVZ; could be extended with namespace control later.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/201455/][XCB: the X Protocol C Binding]]: XCB replaces Xlib as the C binding for X Protocol, offering smaller footprint (26KB vs 750KB), latency hiding, direct protocol access, better threading, and built on XML description (xcb-proto). RC1 of XCB 1.0 provides stable ABI; XCL (Xlib compatibility layer) allows legacy apps to run.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/202449/][API changes: interrupt handlers and vectored I/O]]: Two major 2.6.19 API changes: (1) Removing regs pointer from irq handlers saves stack space (20% IRQ exit speedup on FRV); (2) Adding vectored I/O to AIO by replacing single buffer with iovec array for aio_read()/aio_write(). The readv()/writev() file_operations methods were removed and reimplemented via aio methods.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/202631/][Similar in spirit?]]: Debate over whether GPLv3 anti-DRM provisions violate GPLv2's "similar in spirit" promise. Critics: provisions are new restrictions that fail to stop DRM and could be circumvented by ROM. Supporters: they preserve user freedom to modify software on devices they own. Editor suggests FSF drop anti-DRM language to keep community united.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/202106/][Busy busy busybox]]: BusyBox switched to GPLv2-only after GPLv3 controversy. Mixed licensing ("or any later version" vs "version 2 only" vs BSD) complicated moving to GPLv3. Rob Landley resigned, Denis Vlasenko took over aiming to bring BusyBox to desktop. Bruce Perens forked due to disagreement over license terms and "any later version" treatment.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/204682/][Return values, warnings, and error situations]]: pci_set_mwi() now returns -EINVAL on failure, exposing latent bugs in callers. The __must_check annotation causes warnings when return values are ignored; Alan Cox argued it was wrongly applied to pci_set_mwi() since failures are non-fatal. Andrew Morton proposed that negative error codes should never be ignored; functions returning errors for non-error situations need API redesign.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/204935/][The death and possible rebirth of sysctl()]]: sysctl() system call deprecated in 2.6.19 except for embedded configs. Violates "never break user-space API" promise. Alan Cox protested removal. Linus asked for real-world app breakage examples; none materialized. sysctl() may be restored for normal configs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/205624/][Using sparse for endianness verification]]: Al Viro added endianness annotations (__le16, __le32, __be64 etc.) to catch byte-order mixing bugs. Sparse static checker verifies data annotated with these types isn't incorrectly mixed with native-order values. Run sparse with: make C=2 CF=-D__CHECK_ENDIAN__
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/205644/][GPL-only symbols and ndiswrapper]]: 2.6.19 changed GPL-only symbol checks to test TAINT_PROPRIETARY_MODULE flag rather than just license type. This blocked ndiswrapper from GPL-only symbols even though ndiswrapper is GPL-licensed, because it taints when loading binary NDIS drivers. Alan Cox: EXPORT_SYMBOL_GPL asserts symbols aren't public. Debate over whether NDIS drivers (written for Windows, not Linux) are derivative works.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/206014/][Patch summary: regulatory domains, network channels, and virtualization]]: Three patch sets: (1) Luis Rodriguez's regulatory domain framework for 802.11 to track jurisdiction-specific transmission constraints; (2) Evgeniy Polyakov's netchannel mechanism with netchannel_control() syscall offering NETCHANNEL_CREATE/SEND/RECV operations for user-space network stack; (3) Avi Kivity's /dev/kvm for Intel VT hardware virtualization with ioctl interface for creating VMs, vCPUs, memory slots.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/207094/][Unmaintained free software]]: The unmaintained-free-software.org wiki attempts to connect abandoned projects with willing maintainers. Only 7 projects added in 2006, 2 adopted. Debian's WNPP handles similar packaging-level issues. Infrastructure exists but needs greater awareness.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/207890/][On Novell and Microsoft]]: Analysis of the Novell-Microsoft agreement (November 2006). Microsoft recommends SUSE Linux and provides coupons; companies exchange patent royalty payments (net to Novell). Key legal debate: whether Novell's "covenant not to sue" customers violates GPL Section 7 by creating asymmetric patent arrangements. OIN patents (donated by Novell) may not be covered by the agreement. Microsoft promised not to sue individual developers, but only for non-distribution activities. Community reaction split between viewing it as sellout or pragmatic business move.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/208381/][Review: Linux Administration Handbook, Second Edition]]: Review of 1000-page book by Evi Nemeth et al, derived from Unix Administration Handbook. Covers DNS, email (sendmail/postfix), Samba, backups (Bacula), networking, security. Missing SELinux, udev, wireless, and desktop topics. Still recommended as best combination of technical depth and real-world wisdom for sysadmins.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/208117/][Task watchers]]: Matt Helsley's task watchers patch provides notifications for process lifecycle events (init, clone, exec, uid, gid, exit, free) via ELF section mechanism. Watcher functions are placed in read-only ELF sections at build time to minimize fork() overhead. Can't be unregistered or loaded as modules. Alternative to notifier lists for performance-critical process creation path.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/209175/][Resisting the binary blob]]: Editorial defending Fedora Core's refusal to include proprietary binary blobs. Argues that accepting closed drivers undermines incentives for free drivers, and distributions should be praised (not criticized) for maintaining 100% free systems. Mentions FSF recognition issues with Fedora's packaging guidelines. Community debate over pragmatism vs principles regarding binary firmware/drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/209497/][Some notes on free Java]]: Sun released Java under GPL (following the "Harmony" pattern where free reimplementation prompts original vendor to go GPL). Apache Harmony project continues separately (Apache license incompatible with GPL). GNU Classpath remains relevant for parts Sun can't release. Debate over copyright assignment to Sun and patent concerns. Sun also hinted at releasing Solaris under GPL.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/209301/][Open Firmware is now free]]: Open Firmware (OpenBoot) by Mitch Bradley - Forth-based firmware interpreter - is now completely free under BSD/MIT. Originally written for Sun SPARCstation in 1986. ~350KB, handles filesystems, TCP/IP, USB, device drivers. Now used in OLPC project alongside LinuxBIOS. Can serve as boot loader and provides interactive Forth console for hardware debugging.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/209101/][Counting on the time stamp counter]]: TSC (Time Stamp Counter) provides high-resolution timing but has problems: frequency changes with power management, stops when CPU idles, drifts between SMP processors. Dynamic tick patches conflict with TSC reliability checks. Ingo Molnar argues no working TSC-based gettimeofday exists after 10 years. Proposed solutions include per-CPU offsets/multipliers and conditional dynamic tick disabling when no reliable timer exists.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/209257/][Injecting faults into the kernel]]: Akinobu Mita's fault injection framework (in 2.6.19-rc5-mm2) tests error handling by causing random failures. Configurable via debugfs: slab/page allocation failures, disk I/O failures. Can target specific tasks (via task-filter), address ranges, or GFP flags. Useful for flushing out bugs in error paths that are rarely exercised.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/210272/][Virtual Machines and Memory Protections]]: JIT compilers (Mono, JVM) conflict with PaX/SELinux memory protections that prevent runtime code execution. C# programs can call native libraries (libpng, zlib) with security vulnerabilities. Solutions: AOT compilation (eliminates JIT but breaks dynamic assembly), Ulrich Drepper's double-mapping file technique, or hybrid approaches. Java has same JIT/security tension.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/211279/][Workqueues get a rework]]: David Howells reduced work_struct from 96 to ~32 bytes on 64-bit by removing embedded timer_list, private data pointer, and converting pending bit from full word to bit field. API changed: delayed_work separated from work_struct; work functions now receive work_struct pointer. Added "NAR" (non-auto-release) variants. 2.6.20 merge.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/211505/][Avoiding - and fixing - memory fragmentation]]: Mel Gorman's page clustering (v27) groups pages by type: movable (user processes, __GFP_MOVABLE), reclaimable (kernel caches, __GFP_RECLAIMABLE), non-reclaimable. Achieves 80% memory available as contiguous blocks vs <1% normally. Andy Whitcroft's lumpy reclaim modifies LRU to free neighboring pages together to create higher-order blocks. Combines well with clustering.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/211883/][File-based capabilities]]: Serge Hallyn's patch adds capabilities to executable files via security.capability xattr. On exec, process capability masks set from file's permitted/effective/inheritable masks. Requires root to set xattrs. Can reduce need for setuid-root programs by granting only needed capabilities (e.g., NET_BIND_SERVICE). Concern: works even on nosuid mounts. Could be managed by distributions via rpm/dpkg.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/212724/][The timer API: size or type safety?]]: Two proposals for timer API redesign: (1) Al Viro's type-safe approach using void* callback argument and SETUP_TIMER macro with compile-time type checking via typeof() and expression that verifies callback signature; (2) Thomas Gleixner's size optimization removing data field entirely, passing timer_list* to callbacks and using container_of(). Ingo Molnar argues small size beats type safety. Embedded systems prioritize memory; others prioritize correctness.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/212794/][Secure deletion and trash bin support]]: Nikolai Joukov's ext4 patch implements chattr's undeletable/secure-deletion flags. Deleted files with these flags set move to .trash/<uid>/ instead of removal. User-space daemon handles secure overwrite. Kernel-level trash needed because user-space can't intercept rm/unlink from shells or non-cooperative apps. May be moved to VFS layer for all filesystems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/213672/][Kevent take 26]]: Evgeniy Polyakov's kevent interface (event notification) reaches version 26. Ulrich Drepper pushed for: thread-safe cancellation, user-space ring buffer for zero-syscall event retrieval, absolute timeout support. API: kevent_ctl() for management, kevent_get_events() with timespec timeouts, kevent_init() returns fd, kevent_ring with ring_over counter for wrap detection, kevent_wait() and kevent_commit(). May be renamed to "uevent" to avoid confusion with existing uevent subsystem.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/215075/][Why binary-only modules were not banned]]: Martin Bligh/Andrew Morton proposed banning binary-only modules with 1-year warning; Greg KH created warning patch. Linus Torvalds rejected it. Reasons: GPL doesn't forbid loading proprietary modules, fair use cuts both ways (anti-DMCA tactics rejected), determining derived-work status is complex. GPL covers distribution, not usage. Future: copyright holders may pursue GPL enforcement lawsuits against specific binary module distributors.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/214457/][Reworking NAPI]]: Stephen Hemminger's NAPI rework separates poll/weight from net_device into struct napi_struct. Enables multi-port adapters sharing single NAPI state and poll function. API change: poll(struct napi_struct*, int budget) replaces poll(struct net_device*). Target: 2.6.21.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/215868/][A nasty file corruption bug - fixed]]: Memory-mapped file write corruption bug in ext3 (and other filesystems) where dirty PTE bits got cleared without propagating to buffer heads, causing blocks to be skipped during writeback. Root cause: set_page_dirty() called while filesystem I/O was in progress cleared bh dirty flags incorrectly. Fix: call set_page_dirty() when VM decides to write page, ensuring all buffers marked dirty before writepage(). Linus wants buffer heads eliminated from I/O paths entirely.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/219794/][Network namespaces]]: Container virtualization requires network isolation. Three approaches: (1) L2 namespace (Dmitry Mishin) - per-CPU current namespace stack, virtual Ethernet devices for inter-namespace communication; (2) L3 namespace (Daniel Lezcano) - child of L2, no admin capabilities, complete isolation; (3) Eric Biederman's approach - offset-based per-namespace global variables in kernel sections, explicit namespace parameter everywhere, shadow sysfs directories. All use virtual network devices for cross-namespace traffic.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/220897/][Kernel fibrillation]]: Zach Brown's "fibril" patches - lightweight coroutines for async I/O in kernel. Alan Cox noted they're identical to traditional coroutines. Ingo Molnar prefers kernel thread pools; fibrils currently single-CPU only. Linus: fibrils eliminate separate async I/O code paths and reduce setup/teardown costs (important since most async ops satisfied from cache). async_submit(result_pointer, syscall_number, args) API proposed. Integration with kevent unclear.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/221097/][Review: Linux Kernel in a Nutshell]]: Greg Kroah-Hartman's O'Reilly book (~180 pages) covers kernel configuring, building, installing, PCI driver discovery, command-line parameters, make targets. Concise desktop reference for kernel 2.6.18. Greg made book available online at kroah.com/lkn. Lacks depth on SELinux policy, preemption reliability concerns.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/221115/][Priority-Boosting RCU Read-Side Critical Sections]]: Paul McKenney on rt-RCU priority boosting. Problem: blocked RCU readers can stall grace periods, causing memory buildup. Three approaches explored; first approach (minimizing scheduler-path overhead with locking on non-fastpaths) chosen as correct solution. Avoids atomic instructions, memory barriers, cache misses in common-case read path.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/221940/][Avoiding the tar pit]]: Editorial on how free software avoids large-project tar pits. Key strategies: incremental "pothole fixing" over "boil-the-ocean" rewrites, short release cycles (kernel 2-3 months), independent parallel development teams, peer review, no marketing promises. References Frederick Brooks' "The Mythical Man-Month." Linux avoided 2.4/2.5 tar pit through better development process. Vista's delays seen as industry-wide phenomenon, not Microsoft-specific.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/223839/][KVM 15]]: KVM 15 released with live migration. Process: TCP/SSH connection, iterative memory copy with write protection (dirty page tracking), stop guest when dirty pages below threshold, copy registers/state. ~800 lines in QEMU. Works between Intel and AMD hosts; 64-bit to 32-bit migration not supported. KVM relies on hardware VT, kernel for scheduling/memory management, QEMU for emulation. API frozen for 2.6.21.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/223899/][Threadlets]]: Ingo Molnar's threadlet patches extend syslets. Threadlets: user-space functions run synchronously unless they block, then kernel creates a thread. API: threadlet_fn() must call complete_threadlet_fn(), threadlet_exec(stack, async_head_user) provides stack and completion ring. Hides atom/IO-backend complexity from most developers. Syslets remain for low-level fast paths, threadlets for general use.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/223980/][Thread-based or event-based?]]: Debate between Evgeniy Polyakov (kevents scale better) and Ingo Molnar (threads via scheduler already optimized, easier programming). Event-based: small overhead, good for IO. Thread-based: scheduler heavily optimized, avoids state-machine complexity, any syscall can block naturally. Linus: use both - select/poll/epoll/kevent for events, threadlets/syslets for code execution with blocking.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/225714/][Kernel events without kevents]]: Davide Libenzi proposes converting signals/timers to pollable file descriptors as alternative to kevent: signalfd() creates fd for signals, returns siginfo_t on read; timerfd() with CLOCK_MONOTONIC/REALTIME, TFD_TIMER_REL/ABS/SEQ types, returns count on read. Evgeniy Polyakov's eventfs uses special filesystem but loses signal info. signalfd() competes with normal signal delivery unless sigprocmask() blocks them first. Approach reinforces fd as fundamental kernel object.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/226903/][Linux and flash]]: Status of open source Flash players: swfdec (LGPL, Benjamin Otte) now plays YouTube video - hitting "80/20" point (80% of ads, 20% of content). Gnash focuses on correctness over YouTube specifically. Both LGPL licensed. Competition between projects seen as beneficial (like KDE/GNOME). Also discusses need for Flash authoring tools to bring freedom to Flash development ecosystem.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/226756/][Toward improved page replacement]]: Rik van Riel's patch splits active/inactive lists into separate anonymous and page cache lists. Currently when swappiness is low, kernel scans over unwanted anonymous pages to find page cache pages - wasteful under memory pressure. Separate lists enable targeted eviction without scanning irrelevant pages. Also reduces L2 cache thrashing. Future: may auto-adjust based on measured IO cost rather than swappiness knob.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/226710/][fallocate()]]: Amit Arora's fallocate(fd, mode, offset, len) syscall preallocates disk blocks. mode: FA_ALLOCATE (allocate) or FA_DEALLOCATE (hole punching). Calls filesystem's fallocate() inode op. POSIX posix_fallocate() implementable on top. Allows contiguous allocation, guarantees space before operations, avoids writing filler data. Currently only ext4 has patches; XFS had ioctl preallocation. Generic fallback would require writing zeros.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/228637/][Two approaches to Flash]]: Interview with Gnash (Rob Savoye) and Swfdec (Benjamin Otte) developers. Gnash: GPL, portability/embedded focus, clean room reverse engineering due to Adobe EULA prohibiting Flash developers. Swfdec: LGPL, freedesktop/GNOME integration, YouTube focus. Both ~80% coverage. Neither gets distribution support. Competition beneficial for reverse engineering (different bugs reveal Flash internals). Adobe EULA issue: developers who've installed Adobe tools can't work on Flash implementations.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/229774/][Toward a safer sysfs]]: Tejul Heo's sysfs lifecycle separation patches. Adds s_active rwsem counter tracking active sysfs references; user-space holding sysfs file open doesn't increment counter until actual operation. Cleanup code takes write lock on s_active, blocking new references and waiting for existing ones. Result: kobject can be safely deleted after final kobject_put() without driver worrying about open sysfs files. Also removes owner field from attribute structures - module refcounts unnecessary since driver unload releases devices first.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/229873/][Too many threads]]: SGI 4096-processor system discovers kernel thread scalability problems. Default 32768 process limit fills up with kernel threads. init process parent of all - child list search in wait() becomes O(n) with worst case at tail. stop_machine_run() during module loading creates one thread per CPU, each appended to init's list, making cleanup linear in CPU count. Solutions: move exiting processes to head of list, separate zombie list, detach kernel threads from init, or reduce kernel thread count via on-demand creation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/229984/][The SLUB allocator]]: Christoph Lameter's SLUB replaces slab allocator. Drops per-slab metadata and most queues; free objects tracked via linked list inpage structure (freelist/inuse/offset fields added to struct page). Partial slabs stored per NUMA node. Can merge slabs with similar parameters (50% reduction claimed). Uses RCU for lock-free freeing. Claims 5-10% performance improvement. In -mm tree for 2.6.22.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/230574/][Schedulers: the plot thickens]]: Ingo Molnar's Completely Fair Scheduler (CFS) replaces run queues with single red-black tree tracking all runnable processes. Key: calculates "virtual time" = wait_time / num_processes with priority correction. No time slices, no sleep tracking, no interactive detection. Single tunable: granularity (switch frequency vs throughput). Includes modular scheduler framework (scheduler modules in priority list). Con Kolivas removed SD from consideration; CFS favored for 2.6.22 but likely delayed.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/230975/][ELC: How much memory are applications really using?]]: Matt Mackall's pagemap/kpagemap patches for /proc. Binary pagemap shows page frame number for each virtual address; kpagemap shows mapping count/flags per physical page. Proposes PSS (Proportional Set Size) = pages divided by sharing count, and USS (Unique Set Size) = unshared pages. clear_refs resets referenced bits to measure working set. Tools: memstats, memrank. Embedded Linux Conference presentation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/232575/][UIO: user-space drivers]]: UIO framework lets drivers run partly in user-space. Small kernel module registers device info, memory mappings, interrupt handler. User-space reads /dev/uio0 for events, mmap()s device memory, handles interrupts via poll(). Example: original 5000-line kernel driver + 3000-line userspace reduced to 156 lines kernel + ~3000 userspace. Targets embedded developers who aren't kernel experts. Andrew Morton expressed GPL concerns; authors argue it's better than ad-hoc user-space drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/232757/][Large block size support]]: Christoph Lameter's patch lets page cache hold blocks larger than PAGE_SIZE using compound pages. Enables filesystems to use larger blocks for efficiency. Benefits: better large-file throughput, improved DMA scatter/gather. Concerns: adds VM complexity, requires handling faults on larger pages, increases fragmentation, demands higher-order page allocation. Alternative proposed: keep page cache entry size but allocate entries in higher-order groups.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/235770/][OSBC: The Microsoft/Novell panel]]: Panel debate at Open Source Business Conference 2007. Corbet: agreement bad for FOSS because it acknowledges Microsoft IP claims over Linux code, weakens community defense. Justin Steinman: Microsoft now Novell's biggest sales channel, increased Linux adoption. Allison Randal: mostly irrelevant, just another partnership. Sam Ramji: interoperability focus, Microsoft spends $100M/year defending patents. Audience vote split; jury still out.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/236108/][Slab defragmentation]]: Christoph Lameter's patch addresses internal fragmentation in slab allocator where objects spread across partial pages can't be reclaimed. New kmem_cache_ops: get() pins objects, kick() reallocates them elsewhere to free target page. API changed to accept ops structure. Includes kmem_cache_vacate(page) function. Initial support for inode/dentry caches. Low-overhead way to improve memory utilization.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/236038/][Process containers]]: Paul Menage's containers V10 patch (from Google). Introduces subsystems (resource controllers) and containers (process groups). Mount /dev/container filesystem; subsystems attach to hierarchies. Hierarchical containers allow nested resource control. cpusets becomes a subsystem. tasks file lists/controls container membership. fork()/exit() subsystem callbacks. No syscalls - all management via filesystem. Foundation for CPU time, I/O bandwidth, memory limit controllers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/236843/][Fun with file descriptors]]: Creating separate namespace for internal file descriptors to avoid conflicts with glibc/library usage. Linus proposed using bit 30 in fd number for separate space. Davide Libenzi's patch adds linked-list structure for non-sequential fds with dup2(FD_UNSEQ_ALLOC) to allocate from it. Ulrich Drepper prefers new nonseqfd() syscall over hidden flags. Also proposes O_CLOEXEC flag for open() to fix race where fd could leak across exec between open() and fcntl() calls.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/236920/][The thorny case of kmalloc(0)]]: kmalloc(0) behavior debated: return NULL (confuses error semantics), smallest 8-byte object (allows silent writes), or ZERO_SIZE_PTR (faults on dereference). ZERO_SIZE_PTR chosen - looks like valid pointer but faults if dereferenced. Linus: don't use kmalloc(0) for unique cookies, use kmalloc(1). SLUB unmasked previously-hidden kmalloc(0) callers. Probably won't be in 2.6.23.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/256428/][GNOME and OOXML]]: GNOME Foundation joined ECMA TC45-M (OOXML standardization) to "keep the bastards honest" - Jody Goldberg was doing technical work to ensure OOXML was properly documented. Controversy over community involvement without proper PR announcement. Debate over whether engaging with OOXML standardization helps or harms ODF adoption. Corbet: format exists and must be interoperated with; engagement allows shaping the standard. OpenDocument remains format of choice; KDE clearer on position than GNOME.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/250967/][What every programmer should know about memory, Part 1]]: Ulrich Drepper's seminal memory document (~100 pages, 7 installments). Covers: commodity hardware (Northbridge/Southbridge architecture, RAM types - SRAM vs DRAM), DRAM access protocol (RAS/CAS, precharging, timing), DDR1/2/3 specifications, memory access cycles (200+ for RAM vs 3 for L1d vs 14 for L2). Explains why memory matters and why caches are necessary.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/252125/][Memory part 2: CPU caches]]: Continues Drepper's memory series. Covers CPU cache architecture (L1d/L1i/L2/L3), cache line structure (64 bytes typical), cache operation (tag lookup, dirty flags), MESI cache coherency protocol, associativity (direct-mapped vs set-associative vs fully associative). Shows benchmark results: sequential vs random access patterns, prefetching effects, TLB impact. Key: 4KB L1d, 1MB L2 typical; random access 450+ cycles when working set exceeds cache.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/253361/][Memory part 3: Virtual Memory]]: Virtual memory implementation, multi-level page tables (4-level on x86-64), page table walking. Translation Lookaside Buffer (TLB) - fully associative, small (128 entries), must be flushed on context switch without extended tags. Large pages (2MB/4MB) reduce TLB pressure but cause internal fragmentation. Virtualization impact: shadow page tables vs EPT/NPT (Extended/Nested Page Tables) for hardware-assisted translation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/257209/][Memory part 4: NUMA support]]: NUMA hardware (AMD Opteron integrated memory controller, hypercube topology), Linux NUMA support via libnuma. Memory policies: local allocation vs interleaving. /proc/PID/numa_maps shows page distribution across nodes. Remote memory access costs: 1-hop 9% slower reads, 2-hop 30% slower. Kernel default: memory striping to avoid node exhaustion. sysfs exposes node distances for optimization.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/258154/][Memory part 9: Appendices and bibliography]]: Final installment of Drepper's memory series. Contains: matrix multiplication benchmark code with cache blocking and prefetch intrinsics (_mm_prefetch), branch prediction debugging macros using ELF sections, cache line sharing test (pthread atomic vs regular increments). Includes OProfile usage tips, performance ratios (L1d miss rate, ITLB miss rate), libNUMA API for CPU/memory node topology queries, ECC RAM explanation (Hamming codes, SECDED).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/257417/][Memory management for graphics processors]]: Thomas Hellstrom's TTM (Translation Table Maps) provides GPU memory management for Linux. Buffer objects represent GPU-accessible memory regions; can be moved between VRAM, system RAM, GART. Fence objects track GPU command completion for synchronization. Addresses API problems where X server, kernel, and proprietary drivers each handled GPU memory differently. Aims to replace ad-hoc DRM memory management with proper kernel infrastructure.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/257297/][Process IDs in a multi-namespace world]]: PID namespaces (for 2.6.24) face API problems: PIDs leak across namespace boundaries via realtime signals, SYSV message queues, and robust futexes. Futexes set PID in userspace fast-path - no kernel involvement to translate. Ulrich Drepper wants namespace consolidation; Linus argues PID-based locking across namespaces is like NFS lock files - simply won't work. PID namespaces may be disabled in 2.6.24 to fix API properly.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/257541/][Page replacement for huge memory systems]]: Rik van Riel's patch splits active/inactive page lists into separate anonymous and file-backed lists. Current algorithm scans too many irrelevant pages on large-memory systems (256M pages on 1TB system). Separate lists let VM target specific page types. Future plans: non-reclaimable list for mlock'd pages, working-set tracking, bounded active/inactive list sizes, OOM killer improvements.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/258453/][Google's phone initiative: Android]]: Google's Open Handset Alliance announces Android platform. Uses Linux kernel + Dalvik register-based VM (not JVM, .dex executables), Apache Harmony class libraries. Java ME fragmentation avoided via OHA anti-fragmentation agreement. SDK under restrictive license; source to be released under Apache v2. $10M Android Developer Challenge. Potentially impacts OpenMoko; Java sandbox offers malware protection.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/258550/][Various topics related to kernel quality]]: Andrew Morton's list of ignored bugs triggered debate. Ingo Molnar: need objective metrics, better treatment of testers. Without measurements, quality work is "groping in the dark." Bisection is powerful but some see it as developer laziness. Subsystem mailing lists cause information isolation. Regression tracking should be priority - "if we're chasing regressions, kernel isn't deteriorating."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/258238/][Better per-CPU variables]]: Christoph Lameter's new per-CPU allocator replaces pointer arrays with contiguous per-CPU memory regions. Each CPU gets reserved virtual address range; object offsets are constant, so single pointer + offset finds any CPU's copy. Advantages: one less pointer dereference, better cache utilization, eliminates wasted NR_CPUS pointers, easier CPU hotplug support. Concern: static per-CPU areas bloat kernel data space.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/259217/][PID namespaces in the 2.6.24 kernel]]: OpenVZ/IBM implementation. struct pid now holds array of struct upid (pid value + namespace reference). clone(CLONE_NEWPID) creates namespace; first task becomes init (PID 1) and child reaper. procfs mounted per-namespace to show namespace-local PIDs. Helpers: pid_nr() for global PIDs, pid_vnr() for virtual PIDs as seen by task, pid_nr_ns() for specific namespace. Marked experimental for 2.6.24.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/259157/][GCC unplugged]]: Debate over GCC plugin API. Concerns: proprietary extensions would be too easy, API stability burden. Stallman reportedly blocked plugins. LLVM/clang gaining ground as BSD-licensed alternative. Better documentation vs plugin mechanism argument. Ian Lance Taylor fears GCC irrelevance if can't add unstable API plugins.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/259364/][Piecing together free java]]: One year after Sun's OpenJDK release under GPL. IcedTea project builds OpenJDK into distros (replaces non-free blobs). Requires GCJ to bootstrap. Cacao and HotSpot C++ interpreter enable PowerPC/s390 ports. GNU Classpath still needed for some pieces. Java Liberation Day 2006 freed 96% of OpenJDK code.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260118/][Linux in realtime]]: 9th Real-Time Linux Workshop papers. RT_PREEMPT patches show no significant general-performance impact, latencies approaching commercial RTOS. Multi-core realtime via LITMUSRT (pluggable schedulers). XtratuM nanokernel for virtualization+realtime. Simply faster processor doesn't mean better realtime behavior.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260041/][Tightening symbol exports]]: And Kleen's module symbol namespace patch. EXPORT_SYMBOL_NS(namespace, symbol) restricts symbols to whitelisted modules. Creates tcp/udp/congestion namespaces. Reduces ~30 exported symbols. Rusty Russell and Herbert Xu object: complexity without clear benefit, namespace names redundant when symbols already named consistently.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260068/][kmemcheck]]: Vegard Nossum's patch detects uninitialized memory use. Doubles slab allocations, keeps shadow pages hidden from page tables. Clears present bit so accesses fault. On fault: writes set shadow 0xff, reads check if shadow is zero (uninitialized) and print stack trace. Very slow but useful for debugging kernels.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/255364/][Memory part 5: What programmers can do]]: Continues Drepper's memory series - Section 6. Non-temporal write operations (_mm_stream_*) bypass cache for data written once. Matrix multiplication example: transposing mul2 matrix gives 77% speedup; sub-matrix blocking (cache line-sized tiles) avoids copying; vectorization with SSE2 intrinsics achieves 10x total speedup (318 MFLOPS to 3.35 GFLOPS). Structure padding with pahole tool to fit cache lines. Alignment requirements - 16-byte for SSE. Optimizing for L1d: put frequently-accessed fields first to be critical word.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/256433/][Memory part 6: More things programmers can do]]: Multi-threading cache issues: false sharing when threads modify adjacent variables on same cache line (390-1147% overhead). Solutions: put per-thread variables on separate cache lines, use thread-local storage (__thread). Atomic operations: use atomic arithmetic (__sync_add_and_fetch) not CAS loop (3x slower). Memory bandwidth bottlenecks when many cores share FSB. Thread affinity (sched_setaffinity) to co-locate threads with data. NUMA programming: libnuma, mbind(), set_mempolicy(), MPOL_BIND/PREFERRED/INTERLEAVE policies. get_mempolicy() queries page allocation node.
