- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260817/][Development process latency]]: Corbet editorial on slow release cycles hurting free software. Emacs 22 took 5 years; Xft font support still not in release after years in repo. Linux kernel 3-month cycles contrast sharply. Ingo Molnar: RMS's tight Emacs control limits freedom; 1B lines of FOSS vs 10M in GNU projects. Others argue stable releases help enterprise users avoid constant breakage. Balance between progress and stability debated.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260916/][Book review: Linux System Programming]]: Robert Love's book covering Linux system programming. Good comparisons of select() vs poll() vs epoll(), mmap() vs file I/O. Historical call info for portability. Critics: code examples simplistic, missing race conditions and synchronization details. Best for beginners; experienced programmers get reference material and crystallizing comparisons.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/261003/][Enterprise realtime and cooperative development]]: Red Hat and Novell both shipping forked realtime kernels (400+ patches outside mainline). Financial trading drives enterprise realtime demand - agents need deterministic worst-case latency, not just average speed. Realtime = bounded latency, not fast; hard realtime risks 20-30% throughput loss. GPL enables constant forking and re-merging unlike proprietary Unix. Multimedia (audio recording) also benefits from low-latency kernels.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260832/][Memory access and alignment]]: Daniel Drake's article on unaligned memory access. Natural alignment: N-byte access must align to N-byte boundary. ARM/Alpha: kernel catches exception and fixes access (slow). x86/x86_64: tolerates misalignment but with performance penalty (1 cycle on Opteron). Solutions: byte-wise access, get_unaligned()/put_unaligned() macros, memcpy(). Use __attribute__((packed)) carefully - compiler inserts extra instructions. Linux kernel doc: compare_ether_addr() requires 16-bit alignment.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/260880/][The return of network channels]]: Evgeniy Polyakov's network channels v22. Push packet processing to user space to reduce cache misses and context switches. New syscall: netchannel_control() with NETCHANNEL_CREATE. read()/write() for packet receive/send. Benchmarks: 3x bandwidth, 1/3 CPU for small packets. Large packets: copying overhead dominates so no gain. Needs: filtering, NAT, traffic control hooks before mainline. Zero-copy using network allocator planned.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/261804/][On entropy and randomness]]: Kernel RNG provides /dev/random (blocks when low entropy) and /dev/urandom (never blocks, uses SHA to extend entropy). Ted Ts'o: /dev/urandom cryptographically strong once sufficient entropy; breaking it means breaking SHA. Fresh installs lack entropy - needs better pool initialization with host-specific data (MAC, PCI info). Hardware entropy sources (Via CPU RNG, sound card thermal noise) help servers. rngd daemon feeds /dev/urandom back to /dev/random as workaround.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/262721/][Hardware Fun with the Arduino board]]: Arduino Diecimila ($35, Atmel AVR 8-bit RISC, USB) open-source electronics prototyping platform. Programming via Arduino language (based on Wiring/C/C++). Ubuntu Linux support via avr-gcc, avrdude. IDE built with Java. Shield boards extend with motor control, sensors, XBee, displays. Quick learning curve for hardware projects; interactive sensors and actuators. Author found LED blinker and button input examples easy to get working.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/262554/][Short subjects: kerneloops, read-mostly, and port 80]]: Three kernel topics: (1) kerneloops.org - Arjan van de Ven's site aggregating kernel oops from mailing lists, correlating by kernel version/call trace to find most common crashes. Needs UUID marker at end of oops for reliable parsing. (2) __read_mostly grouping: groups rarely-changed variables into shared cache lines to reduce SMP bouncing. Andrew Morton warns over-use could pack write-heavy variables together and increase cache line bouncing. Suggests "read_often" attribute instead. (3) I/O port delays using port 80 write causing hard freezes on some x86_64 systems. udelay() alternative being adopted, but bootstrap timing calibration problem remains.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/262464/][What is RCU, Fundamentally?]]: Paul McKenney's detailed explanation of Read-Copy-Update synchronization. Three mechanisms: (1) Publish-Subscribe: rcu_assign_pointer() publishes, rcu_dereference() subscribes - enforces ordering for compiler/CPU. (2) Wait for readers: synchronize_rcu() waits for pre-existing RCU read-side critical sections (rcu_read_lock/unlock) to complete via context-switch trick. (3) Multiple versions: maintains old versions during grace periods so concurrent readers see consistent data. Works on non-preempt kernels with zero overhead. Examples with linked lists showing deletion and replacement. seqlock differs: readers must retry if concurrent writer.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/254740/][The future of AppArmor]]: Novell laid off AppArmor dev team; former members formed Mercenary Linux for consulting. Novell still committed to AppArmor as SUSE Enterprise security. AppArmor originally proprietary (Immunix), GPLed 2006, submitted to kernel. Criticism: path-based vs inode-based security policy. Advantage: simpler than SELinux. Ubuntu and Mandriva adding AppArmor support. Goal: merge into mainline kernel. Hurdles both technical (path-based design conflicts) and political. Linus's commitment to keep LSM API removes one barrier.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/254711/][Fair user scheduling and other scheduler patches]]: CFS group scheduling merged for 2.6.24 without control groups. Per-user fair scheduling: creates implicit group per user, each gets fair CPU share. Tunable via /sys/kernel/uids/<uid>/cpu_share (default 1024, ratio determines CPU allocation). Steven Rostedt/Gregory Haskins patches: realtime task migration to available CPUs when current CPU has higher-priority task. Avoids cache-cold migration cost vs leaving realtime task waiting. Peter Zijlstra's hi-res preemption tick: uses high-resolution timer to preempt exactly at time slice end, avoids overrunning and补偿. Reduces context switching, improves throughput.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/255529/][Various topics, all related to interrupts]]: (1) Jeff Garzik wants to remove irq argument from handler prototype - most handlers don't use it, dev_id suffices. Auditing all in-tree handlers for cleanup. (2) Alexey Dobniyan's irq_flags_t type for spin_lock_irqsave() flags parameter - type-safe, lets sparse catch int vs unsigned long bugs. Andrew Morton reluctant due to massive churn. (3) synchronize_irq() race: memory write reordering could make flag change invisible to IRQ handler after synchronize returns. Fix: acquire descriptor lock at end of wait loop - locked operation forces memory barrier synchronization between processors. Linus/Herbert Xu patch ensures proper ordering.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/237722/][More fun with file descriptors]]: Problem: open() flags (O_CLOEXEC, non-sequential FD) can't be set for socket(), pipe(), epoll_create(), accept() etc. Solutions considered: (1) socket2() syscall with flags parameter - rejected as syscall proliferation. (2) prctl(PR_SPARSEFD) global flag - breaks POSIX guaranteed lowest-available-FD behavior; libraries can't safely enable globally. (3) Linus's syscall_indirect(): single syscall to invoke any other with specified flags and signal mask. Could implement pselect() variants, control symlink handling, async syslets. Concerns: flag bit exhaustion, no compile-time checking of flag applicability. No working implementation yet.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/237924/][KHB: Real-world disk failure rates]]: Two USENIX FAST papers studying 100,000+ disks. Schroeder/Gibson (CMU/HPC): real failure rate 3% avg vs 0.5-0.9% manufacturer estimate; one batch had 13.5% rate. No correlation with disk type - SATA as reliable as SCSI/FC. Debunked "bathtub model" - failure rate increases steadily, no sweet spot. Batch correlation: disk failures correlated up to 2 years later (bad for RAID from same batch). Google study (Pinheiro/Weber/Barroso): 1.7-8.6% failure rate. Surprisingly, high temperature negatively correlated with failure - cooler drives fail more. SMART errors correlate with failure: scan errors -> 30% fail within 8 months, reallocation errors -> 15%. But 36% of drives fail with NO warning.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/238948/][Getting the message from the kernel]]: Proposal to add unique IDs to kernel printk messages for translation/manual lookup. Japanese users need better kernel message support. Scheme: #define KMSG_COMPONENT + message number (KMSG_ERR(100)), requires kerneldoc for each message. Developer opposition: too much doc overhead, David Miller would stop writing useful messages. Andrew Morton counter-proposal: add message ID externally, maintain database via "kernel messages team" - developers do nothing. Still gaps: who funds the team. Alternative: __FILE__/__LINE__ already pinpoints code; hash of message string; gettext-style translation in userspace logger.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/238923/][btrfs and NILFS]]: Two new filesystems. Btrfs (Chris Mason, Oracle): extent-based, COW, checksums everything, snapshots as subvolumes. Subvolumes have own directory/quota. No separate journaling - transactions via snapshots. Single mutex currently (bad for many CPUs). Missing: defragmentation, direct I/O, mmap write, xattrs, async I/O, OOM handling. NILFS2 (log-structured): circular buffer, writes always at end. Continuous snapshots for "rm -r" recovery. Performance: great for writes, less for reads. TODO: x86_64 support, extent handling.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/239633/][Eliminating tasklets]]: Steven Rostedt proposes removing tasklets entirely. Tasklets run as software interrupts (higher priority than processes), create unbounded latency. Workqueues use dedicated processes, can sleep, be preempted - better. His patch replaces tasklets with workqueue wrapper to avoid rewriting all tasklet users. Developer consensus: ditch wrapper, convert directly to workqueues. Threaded interrupt handlers (all IRQs as kernel threads) coming from realtime tree - will be config option. Ingo Molnar asks: anyone with tasklets in performance-sensitive code should speak up now; rest can be mass-converted.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/239962/][Linux security non-modules and AppArmor]]: James Morris proposes making LSM static (no modules) - unloadable security modules create incoherent state. Most complaints addressed, likely to go in. AppArmor update still path-based (争议): critics say paths are insecure - renames break security, pathname lookup traverses backwards. Andrew Morton: either merge it as "object lesson" or leave it out and force SUSE to maintain out-of-tree. Core dispute: SELinux is architectural framework (policy-flexible, can do AppArmor-style rules); AppArmor is simpler but fundamentally limited. Path-based security can't protect against hard links between protected and unprotected paths.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/240253/][OLS: Three talks on power management]]: Len Brown's Ottawa Linux Symposium talks. (1) Tickless improvements: deferrable timers, round_jiffies() coordinated timer expiry boosted avg sleep 1ms->20ms; HPET timer handling pushed sleep to 74ms avg. (2) ACPI myths debunked: APM is dead (Vista dropped it); suspend-to-disk mostly not ACPI; hotkeys are vendor-specific; more C-states don't mean better power savings; throttling wastes energy (race-to-idle is better). (3) Thermal management: ACPI 2.0 thermal zones with trip points; kernel just conduits events to userspace via netlink/sysfs; each device needs own management daemon. User-space still biggest power consumer - powertop helps identify culprits.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/240474/][CFS group scheduling]]: Ingo Molnar's CFS v18 adds group scheduling (fairness between process groups, not just processes). Srivatsa Vaddagiri's patches: sched_entity encapsulates per-process info; hierarchical scheduling entities with parent pointers and embedded run queues. Example: Alice's X server (1 process) vs Karl's 49 compile jobs - each user group gets 50% CPU. Integration with process containers (cpuctl filesystem): create directories for groups, move processes via container interface. Default: each process scheduled individually. Currently only single-level hierarchy; multi-level possible later. Not ready for 2.6.23, maybe 2.6.24.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/240571/][The ongoing fallocate() story]]: fallocate() preallocation syscall still not in mainline. S/390 architecture constraints required parameter shuffling. Current mode flags: FA_ALLOCATE (allocate and extend file size), FA_RESV_SPACE (allocate without changing size), FA_DEALLOCATE/FA_UNRESV_SPACE (difference: UNRESV returns zeros on read after deallocation). Proposed flags: FALLOC_ZERO_SPACE (mark range uninitialized instead of writing zeros), FALLOC_MKSWAP (privileged, create swap file quickly without zeroing blocks - for OOM emergency), FALLOC_FL_ERR_FREE (rollback on partial allocation). Christoph Hellwig objects: should be minimal posix_fallocate only; block deletion is separate syscall. Bikeshedding noted.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/239238/][An API for virtual I/O: virtio]]: Rusty Russell's virtio abstraction for hypervisor-guest I/O. Separates guest driver from hypervisor-specific code to avoid driver proliferation. Buffers are struct scatterlist arrays with "out" (write to hypervisor) and "in" (read from hypervisor) entries. Operations: add_buf(), sync() (kick), get_buf() (retrieve completed), detach_buf() (cancel), restart() (re-enable callbacks). Block driver: protocol with sector/type info and status. Network driver: separate virtqueues for TX/RX to avoid locking. Draft IV being reviewed; lguest example implementation exists. Performance vs abstraction tradeoff: too much indirection kills performance.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/241980/][USB device authorization]]: Iñaky Perez-Gonzalez's wireless USB security patches. Adds wusb, authorized, authenticated flags to usb_device. Unauthorized devices: kernel won't even read configuration (can't access endpoints until authorized). Sysfs 'authorized' attribute for userspace daemons to set permissions. Per-host authorized_default to globally disable new device connections. Could whitelist device types (keyboards OK, storage blocked) or require signed certificates. Wired USB can also be controlled. Authentication/encryption remains in userspace. Not ready for 2.6.23 merge window.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/242107/][Yet another approach to software suspend]]: Ying Huang's kexec-based hibernation approach builds on kdump infrastructure. Instead of current hibernation code, use secondary "dump kernel" in reserved memory to save system state and jump back to original kernel on resume. Andrew Morton proposed this in 2006 - kdump already stops activity and writes state; just need way to return. Current patch only implements jumping back to original kernel. Benefits: unifies kdump and hibernation, removes process freezer and much current hibernate code. Challenges: devices must enter quiet "hibernate state" not full power-down; on-demand secondary kernel loading; fast resume (two kernel boots unacceptable). User-space can handle image save/restore. Driver issues remain same. Positive initial response but long road ahead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/243795/][Controlling memory use in containers]]: Balbir Singh/Pavel Emelianov's container memory controller (v4). Uses resource counter abstraction with integer values for limits/usage. Tracks RSS and page cache per-container. struct meta_page links pages to container via LRU lists for reclaim. Adds 5 pointers per active page (meta_page in struct page plus 4 in meta_page itself) - significant cost. On OOM, container-specific OOM killer restricts damage to one container. Shared pages charged to first container; reclaim may spread charges across containers. Could limit background jobs (updatedb, backups) or isolate OpenOffice from memory. Paper: "Challenges with the memory controller" explains why separate meta_page structure rather than extending struct page directly (proportional to memory in system).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/243949/][Large receive offload]]: Jan-Bernd Themann's LRO patch for incoming packet aggregation (no hardware needed). Drivers provide net_lro_mgr structure with get_skb_header/get_frag_header to locate IP/TCP headers. lro_receive_skb() aggregates packets into larger fragmented SKBs; lro_receive_frags() for page-based drivers. lro_flush_all() sends accumulated packets; lro_flush_pkt() flushes individual streams. Reduces per-packet overhead in network stack by cutting packet count. max_desc limits simultaneous TCP streams (linear search); max_aggr limits packets per aggregated packet. Planned for 2.6.24 merge. Similar to TSO but for receive side.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/243704/][i386 and x86_64: back together?]]: Thomas Gleixner/Ingo Molnar's 9MB patch merging i386 and x86_64 architectures into new unified arch/x86 tree. 1764 files touched, ~1000-2000 commits planned for bisectable transition. Identical files merged; differing files get _32/_64 suffixes (ioport_32.c, ioport_64.c). Goal: easier bug fixes across both, shared development, cleaner abstraction for virtualization. Andi Kleen opposes: arch/x86_64 is cleaner/legacy-free, splitting allows keeping old junk confined to i386. General consensus favors merge despite maintainer objection. PowerPC 32/64-bit merge in 2.6.15 cited as successful precedent.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/244595/][Tripping over trip points]]: Len Brown's response to complaints about 2.6.22 removing ACPI thermal trip-point override capability. Users wanted to change BIOS-set temperature thresholds for fan/throttling. Len argues: kernel can't change hardware trip points, only disable them and poll manually - risk of overheating if polling fails. Real issues: dust in fans (4 people in bug report), broken user-space power daemons, wrong ACPI implementations. Windows doesn't override trip points either, so Linux should fix real problems instead. Challenge: anyone with legitimate trip-point override use case should submit documented bug report. Fan control via user-space daemon recommended instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/244640/][Newer, newer NAPI]]: NAPI polling interface rework for 2.6.24. Key changes: napi_struct allocated separately (not part of net_device), drivers can create multiple NAPI instances per device for multi-queue hardware. netif_napi_add() registers poll method and weight. poll() prototype now takes budget parameter controlling packets to process; drivers manage quota internally. netif_rx_schedule/_complete replace old functions. napi_enable/disable for polling state. No netif_poll_enable/disable (tied to net_device). netif_napi_remove() doesn't exist - no need. David Miller: API final, just bug fixes before 2.6.24.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/244829/][Once upon atime]]: Ingo Molnar: atime updates are Linux's biggest I/O performance problem, more than all pagecache speedups combined. Every file read causes filesystem write to update atime - kills read-heavy workloads. Solutions: noatime mount option (breaks mail tools, tmpwatch, some backups); relatime (2.6.20) updates atime only if older than mtime - compromise but confuses "accessed in last week" queries. Linus suggested: update atime if current value >N seconds old (e.g., 1 day). Ingo's patch: relatime_interval boot option + default_relatime to enable globally. Proposed: update atime when inode written anyway; Alan Cox objected for predictability.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/245533/][timerfd() and system call review]]: timerfd() added in 2.6.22, returns FD for timer events instead of signals. 2.6.22 bug: read() returned only 1 byte instead of 4 bytes. Also lacks timer remaining query (timerfd() can't tell you how much time is left). Michael Kerrisk's patch adds outmr parameter to return previous timer value. incompatible API change but no glibc release yet so few apps affected. Real problem: system calls added without adequate review. Suggestion: require complete documentation before merging user-space APIs; documentation reveals design flaws. Kernel summit topic: formal review process for APIs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/245671/][Kernel markers]]: Mathieu Desnoyers' marker patch enables static probe points for tracing (SystemTap/LTTng). trace_mark(name, format_string, ...) places probe; format describes arguments. marker_probe_register() attaches probe function; marker_arm() enables it. Problem: markers must have near-zero overhead when not armed (production kernels). Solution: "immediate values" - architecture-specific code patches read operations to embed setting directly, avoiding cache misses. Currently i386/powerpc. Disarm/enable nesting supported. Enables standardized trace points throughout kernel code following source changes. SystemTap example: probe kernel.mark("name") { println($arg1) }.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/245695/][The anatomy of a Linux distribution]]: Rebecca Sobol's overview: Linux distribution = kernel + package set. Over 500 distributions now. Hardware constraints shape kernels (embedded, various archs). Common base distributions: Debian (most common), Red Hat derivatives, Ubuntu. Live CDs (Knoppix) spawned many spin-offs. New trend: Fedora spins, Ubuntu flavors, customizable distributions via maturing tools. Consolidation around few base distributions for customized desktops likely.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/248127/][OOXML loses a battle]]: Microsoft's OOXML failed to get ISO fast-track approval (Sept 2007 vote). Required 2/3 P-member yes, <1/4 no votes. Result: 53% P-yes, 74% P+O yes - failed both tests. Issues: 6000+ page spec, ballot stuffing in Sweden (Microsoft offered "marketing assistance" for $2500 entrance fee), 11 countries upgraded to P-members just before vote. ~10,000 comments to address by Feb 2008 BRM. ODF (ISO-approved) has few comments, open implementations. OOXML critics: contradicts other ISO standards, implements buggy compatibility rather than clean design. Microsoft's tactic seen as "extend and extinguish" lock-in scheme.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/247788/][LinuxConf.eu: Documentation and user-space API design]]: Two talks: (1) Michael Kerrisk - writing man pages reveals bugs (inotify IN_ONESHOT never worked, splice() caused unkillable hangs, timerfd() API problems). Good API traits: simplicity, ease of use, generality, consistency. Example bad APIs: dnotify (signals, required open FD preventing unmount), mlock() vs remap_file_pages() rounding不一致. Proposal: formal signoff with design review, docs, test suite before merging. (2) Arnd Bergmann - "how not to design kernel interfaces". ioctl() not type-safe but good for prototyping; sockets would never get into kernel now (inconsistent with everything); netlink and wireless extensions criticized. Sysfs/proc as dumping grounds. Data structures should match kernel/user-space; avoid pointers, padding issues.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/248180/][The many faces of fsck]]: Valerie Aurora's comprehensive overview. fsck tasks: find consistent filesystem, check metadata (free/allocated bitmaps, link counts, directory cycles). Journaling filesystems (ext3, XFS, ReiserFS) eliminated need for fsck after crashes but still need corruption checking. fsck meanings: (1) check and repair half-finished writes (original UNIX), (2) for soft updates: find leaked inodes/blocks, (3) for COW filesystems (ZFS, WAFL): only corruption check. XFS has xfs_check/xfs_repair; ReiserFS had three modes. Bad old days: operator ran fsck by hand after sync. Loopback mounted disk images corrupted ReiserFS during tree rebuild (magic number false positives). Modern filesystems still need repair capability despite "always consistent" claims.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/249246/][Exported symbols and the internal API]]: Adrian Bunk's patch to un-export sys_open() and sys_read() - wavefront sound driver last in-tree user, being converted to firmware API. Andrew Morton wants EXPORT_UNUSED_SYMBOL() for one release cycle as warning before removal. Others argue: these have been on chopping block for years, warnings don't work (users don't see them), bloats kernel. Morton: don't want to "unnecessarily upset users" with out-of-tree modules. He has power to enforce this; consensus is to just go along. Debate about proper API lifecycle management.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/250468/][Exploiting symlinks and tmpfiles]]: Jake Edge on symlink/tmpfile race conditions. Attack: create symlink in shared directory (like /tmp) pointing to sensitive file (/etc/passwd) before vulnerable privileged program accesses predictable filename. Use O_CREAT|O_EXCL for atomic file creation, mkstemp(), or avoid shared writable directories. Problem: 20+ years old but still common. fopen() has no atomic creation equivalent - must use open()+fdopen(). O_EXCL|O_CREAT broken on NFSv3 - use link() based locking instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/250335/][Large pages, large blocks, and large problems]]: VM mini-summit issues. Problem: 4KB page size hasn't changed while RAM grew 1000x; causes TLB misses, memory fragmentation, I/O inefficiency. Solutions: (1) Mel Gorman's fragmentation avoidance (movable allocations, lumpy reclaim) - some in 2.6.23. (2) Christoph Lameter's large block size patches - requires higher-order page allocation, no fallback, needs warnings if merged. (3) Nick Piggin's fsblock - replaces buffer_heads, supports large blocks via vmap() fallback. (4) Andrea Arcangeli's CONFIG_PAGE_SHIFT - cluster hardware pages into larger software pages. Internal fragmentation (wasted memory) is main tradeoff. Linus called discussion "idiotic"; large block patches may go in with warnings.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/250480/][A generic tracing interface]]: David Wilder's trace layer for debugfs. Kernel side: trace_setup() creates relay channels, trace_start/stop(), probe functions check trace_running() then output via relay. User side: debugfs files for state control, trace0 for data, dropped file for lost records. Works with kprobes or kernel markers. Part of SystemTap infrastructure, helps get tracing into mainline.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/251573/][Reviving linux-tiny]]: CELF-backed revival of linux-tiny patches (reduced kernel footprint for embedded). Tim Bird announced, Michael Opdenacker as new maintainer. Patches being forward-ported from 2.6.14 to 2.6.22. Andrew Morton offered to manage patches for mainline inclusion. printk() strings consume 5-10% kernel size - proposals: severity-based filtering (remove KERN_DEBUG by default), DoPrintk patch (per-file selection), message ID lookup tables (unique per build, strings in separate file). printk() changes controversial but seen as low-hanging fruit for size reduction.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/252794/][What chroot() is really for]]: chroot() just changes root for path lookups, doesn't isolate or sandbox processes. Root can escape via "chdir('..')". BSD jail is mini-virtualization, chroot() is not. Legitimate uses: running daemons (bind/named, Apache) with limited filesystem access, install programs, older libraries. NOT a security tool - exploited code can escalate privileges. POSIX doesn't specify chroot(). Proposed fix to set CWD to new root violates POSIX. Recommended security alternatives: SELinux, AppArmor, namespaces, or full virtualization (OpenVZ/Linux-VServer).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/252716/][What's in the realtime tree]]: 2.6.23-rc8-rt1 has ~400 patches for realtime preemption. Core: realtime mutexes (replace spinlocks, preemptible, priority inheritance). Threaded interrupt handlers - run handlers in kernel threads, easier bug finding. Preemptible RCU - avoids disabling preemption in read-side critical sections. Lockless pagecache (Nick Piggin) - reduces lock contention. kmap() locking removal for high memory. SLUB allocator replaced with per-CPU locked slab allocator for realtime. tools for latency measurement already in mainline.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/252562/][SMACK meets the One True Security Module]]: SMACK v3 posted for 2.6.24 merge. Andrew Morton: "merge it and see if people use it." SELinux developers oppose - having two LSM modules makes removing LSM impossible. James Morris: LSM prevents security from being "first class citizen". Linus: LSM stays, no consensus on security approaches. SMACK (mandatory access control, labels on processes/files) designed for simplicity. Fedora/RHEL made SELinux usable for common cases but still too complex for most. No Single True Security Module for Linux foreseeable.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/253588/][Linux in consumer electronics]]: Sony, other companies embed Linux in cameras, TVs, audio gear, professional video. Embedded advantages: no per-device royalties, virtual memory (overcommit helps 10MB RAM budget apps), hardware driver support, many CPU architectures. Cost: larger kernels - linux-tiny patches help. CELF (Consumer Electronics Linux Forum) coordinates efforts. Companies contribute back (VW for CAN bus, Sony for kernel patches). Upgrades typically via service centers, not user-accessible. Many devices use uClibc instead of glibc for size.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/253425/][PF_CAN]]: Controller Area Network (CAN) - embedded/automotive networking with 8-byte payloads, error-checking, differential signaling. PF_CAN socket API proposal from Volkswagen developers (Urs Thuermann, Oliver Hartkopp). Makes CAN look like regular network sockets - filters, queuing, QoS. Previous implementations just made CAN look like serial ports. VW uses in research vehicles, not production yet. Potential: infotainment, car-to-car communications, diagnostics. Inclusion in mainline likely after 2.6.24.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/253726/][Playing with printk()]]: Vegard Nossum's printk() enhancements from linux-tiny discussion. Goal: reduce kernel size by removing low-priority messages. Original patch: new kprint() infrastructure with separate argument encoding for translation (separating format from args). Rejected as too complex. Simpler version: marker characters (0x1f) around arguments for user-space translation parsing. Jan Engelhardt's colored printk() output (per loglevel colors) merged - small patch, default unchanged.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/253651/][The design of preemptible read-copy-update]]: Paul McKenney's detailed explanation of preemptible RCU for -rt patchset. Classic RCU disables preemption in read side; preemptible RCU allows preemption and blocking on locks. Uses per-CPU rcu_flipctr array with two counters (old/new) tracking read-side critical sections across grace periods. Four-state grace-period machine: idle -> waitack -> waitzero -> waitmb -> idle. rcu_read_lock() increments appropriate counter, records index; rcu_read_unlock() decrements matching counter. Memory barriers moved to grace-period boundaries instead of per-read-side critical section - major performance win for frequent RCU readers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/203924/][The Video4Linux2 API: an introduction]]: Jonathan Corbet's series on writing V4L2 drivers. V4L2 merged in 2.5.46 (Nov 2002). Supports: video capture (cameras, tuners), video output, video overlay, VBI (raw/sliced), radio. Device configuration via many ioctl() calls. Three I/O methods: read/write, video overlay, streaming (via memory-mapped or user-space buffers). Driver must expose device capabilities and negotiate formats with applications.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/204545/][Video4Linux2 part 2: registration and open()]]: V4L2 driver structure. struct video_device holds all driver info. Fields: name, type (VFL_TYPE_GRABBER/VBI/RADIO/VTX), type2 (capability flags like VID_TYPE_CAPTURE), minor. video_register_device() allocates minor number. Must implement file_operations: open(), release(), read()/write(), poll(), mmap(), ioctl() (use video_ioctl2()). Need release() callback for safe kfree after all filehandles close. Devices support multiple open file descriptors for separate configuration/capture.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/206765/][Video4Linux2 part 3: Basic ioctl() handling]]: 79 possible ioctl callbacks; drivers implement needed subset. vidioc_querycap() - mandatory, returns driver name, card description, capabilities (V4L2_CAP_VIDEO_CAPTURE, STREAMING, READWRITE, etc). Use video_ioctl2() or unlocked_ioctl() for dispatch. vivi virtual driver (drivers/media/video/vivi.c) as reference implementation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/213798/][Video4Linux2 part 4: inputs and outputs]]: Video standards (NTSC/PAL/SECAM variants) via v4l2_std_id bitmask. vidioc_s_std() programs hardware for standard. Inputs: vidioc_enum_input() returns name, type (TUNER/CAMERA), audioset, tuner index, supported standards, status flags. vidioc_g_input()/vidioc_s_input() get/set active input. Outputs mirror inputs with similar callbacks: V4L2_OUTPUT_TYPE_MODULATOR/ANALOG/ANALOGVGAOVERLAY. Drivers must initialize current_norm at startup.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/218798/][Video4Linux2 part 5a: colors and formats]]: Colorspaces: V4L2_COLORSPACE_SRGB (RGB, YUV, YCbCr) and V4L2_COLORSPACE_SMPTE170M (analog TV). Pixel formats: packed (all components contiguously) or planar (separate Y/U/V arrays). Fourcc codes identify formats. RGB formats: RGB332, RGB444, RGB555, RGB565, BGR24, RGB24, BGR32, RGB32, SBGGR8 (Bayer pattern from camera sensors). YUV formats: GREY, YUYV, UYVY, Y41P, and planar YUV420/YVU420/YUV410/YVU410. struct v4l2_pix_format has width, height, pixelformat, field (interlacing), bytesperline, sizeimage, colorspace.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/227533/][Video4Linux2 part 5b: format negotiation]]: Application queries supported formats via vidioc_enum_fmt_cap(). vidioc_g_fmt_cap() returns current format. vidioc_try_fmt_cap() checks if format possible without changing hardware. vidioc_s_fmt_cap() sets format (must be idle, returns -EBUSY otherwise). Driver adjusts size if exact size unsupported. Format change is atomic - all or nothing. Compressed formats indicated by V4L2_FMT_FLAG_COMPRESSED.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/235023/][Video4Linux2 part 6a: Basic frame I/O]]: I/O methods: read()/write() (set V4L2_CAP_READWRITE), streaming via buffers (V4L2_CAP_STREAMING). Async I/O not implemented. read()/write() naive implementation: start capture, deliver frame, stop. More advanced: speculative capture with buffering. VIDIOC_G_PARM/VIDIOC_S_PARM control frame rate (v4l2_fract for timeperframe), capturemode (V4L2_MODE_HIGHQUALITY), readbuffers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/240667/][Video4Linux2 part 6b: Streaming I/O]]: Buffers: V4L2_MEMORY_MMAP (kernel-allocated, mmap to user), V4L2_MEMORY_USERPTR (user-allocated). vidioc_reqbufs() allocates buffers. vidioc_querybuf() returns buffer info including m.offset for mmap. Buffer states: queued (incoming), done (outgoing), user-space. vidioc_qbuf()/vidioc_dqbuf() enqueue/dequeue. poll() required for streaming. vidioc_streamon()/vidioc_streamoff() start/stop DMA. Direct DMA to user-space requires scatterlist, get_user_pages(), contiguous pages; video-buf layer helps. v4l2_buffer has timestamp, sequence number, field (interlace), timecode, bytesused.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/247126/][Video4Linux2 part 7: Controls]]: Controls adjust hardware parameters (brightness, contrast, saturation, etc). Standard IDs: V4L2_CID_BRIGHTNESS, V4L2_CID_CONTRAST, etc. Private controls start at V4L2_CID_PRIVATE_BASE. vidioc_queryctrl() returns control info: type (INTEGER/BOOLEAN/MENU/BUTTON), minimum/maximum/step, default_value, flags (DISABLED/GRABBED/READ_ONLY/UPDATE/INACTIVE/SLIDER). vidioc_querymenu() enumerates menu items for menu-type controls. vidioc_g_ctrl()/vidioc_s_ctrl() get/set control values. Extended controls for MPEG encoding via separate interface.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/263554/][The future of unencrypted web traffic]]: ISPs manipulating web traffic (adding content, selling clickstream data) drive push for HTTPS. Rogers Internet tested injecting content into Google homepage for bandwidth-limited users. Comcast interfering with BitTorrent traffic. Solutions: HTTPS encryption prevents snooping/modification but certificate authorities costly, server CPU overhead for encryption. SNI (Server Name Indication) enables name-based virtual hosting with HTTPS. DNSSEC and certificate forests proposed. Regulatory alternatives discussed - making ISPs common carriers liable for modified content. Copyright law may also apply to modified pages as derivative works.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/263130/][What is RCU? Part 2: Usage]]: Paul McKenney's detailed article on RCU usage patterns. Six perspectives on RCU: (1) Reader-writer lock replacement - RCU 100x faster on 16 CPUs, no deadlock cycles, priority-inversion immune. (2) Restricted reference-counting - grace periods prevent deletion while readers active. (3) Bulk reference-counting - avoids cache-line bouncing of global counters. (4) Poor man's garbage collector - timing automatic but manual eligibility marking. (5) Existence guarantees - RCU-protected data survives for duration of read-side critical section. (6) Wait for things to finish - synchronize_sched() waits for pre-existing non-preemptible code (including NMI handlers). Examples with list deletion showing lock-based vs RCU-based code.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/263610/][The Linux Libertine Open Fonts Project]]: Libertine Open Fonts Project (Philipp H. Poll) - GPL/OFL licensed serif font family. Supports TTF and OTF formats, LaTeX. Version 2.7.9 added hinting for Microsoft Word compatibility. Includes FontForge source files. OFL clarifies embedding rights in PDFs. Contains ligature glyphs (fi, ffi, fl, ffl, Th) with OpenType features working in Adobe InDesign. FLOSS fonts still catching up to Microsoft/Apple font technology - OpenType features, non-Latin script support not fully implemented in Free text layouters.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/264490/][Hiding open ports with shimmer]]: shimmer project hides services (SSH, web) using cryptographic port selection. Shared secret + time + service name -> SHA-256 hash -> AES encryption -> 16 ports (1 good, 15 blacklist). Ports change each minute; 3-minute window for clock skew. Vulnerability: attacker observing traffic sees correct port; IP spoofing can bypass blacklist; dependent services (like auth) still exposed. Port knocking alternative. Provides psychological security more than real protection - limited entropy (4-16 bits), only obscures rather than secures.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/264090/][RCU part 3: the RCU API]]: Paul McKenney's comprehensive RCU API reference. RCU API families: RCU Classic (original), RCU BH (DDoS protection), RCU Sched (hardirqs/NMIs), Realtime RCU (preemptible), SRCU (sleeping readers), QRCU (nanosecond grace periods). Read-side primitives: rcu_read_lock/unlock, rcu_read_lock_bh/unlock_bh, preempt_disable/enable. Update primitives: synchronize_rcu(), synchronize_sched(), synchronize_srcu(), synchronize_qrcu(), call_rcu(), call_rcu_bh(), rcu_barrier(). Publish-subscribe primitives: list_for_each_entry_rcu(), list_add_rcu(), list_del_rcu(), list_replace_rcu(), hlist_for_each_entry_rcu(), rcu_dereference(), rcu_assign_pointer(). Multiple RCU implementations coexist in kernel (RCU Classic + Realtime RCU share API).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/265239/][Making code reviews easier with Review Board]]: Review Board - web-based code review application from VMware. Side-by-side diff view with syntax highlighting, comments linked to line numbers. AJAX interface for annotating code sections; supports screenshots of UI with annotations. Inter-diff between patch revisions. Dashboard tracks pending reviews and reviewers. Subversion and Perforce support; git/Mercurial in development. MIT license, Python/Django. Other tools: Codestriker (Perl), Atlassian Crucible (commercial).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/264980/][Ten-year timeline, part 2: the bubble days]]: LWN history 1999-2000 dotcom bubble. VA Linux IPO (record first-day gain), LinuxOne scam, Red Hat acquires Cygnus. Atipa, Linux Mall acquisition offers declined. LWN acquired by Tucows in April 2000. LinuxCare IPO attempt with "the barn" office, $50-60 pre-IPO share offers. ZDNet offer declined. Key events: SourceForge launch, OpenSSH start, BitKeeper non-free license, VA Linux acquires Andover.Net.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/265257/][A better btrfs]]: Btrfs v0.10 improvements: back references on all objects enabling "which file owns this block?" queries, online filesystem resizing (grow/shrink while mounted), in-place ext3-to-btrfs converter keeping ext3 as fallback subvolume, copy-on-write toggle via mount option for database workloads. Still crashes on out-of-space. Future: storage pools spanning multiple devices, striping/mirroring within filesystem, per-directory snapshots, fine-grained locking, incremental backup, online fsck.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/265285/][ext3 metaclustering]]: Abhishek Rai's patch groups indirect blocks at end of each block group ("metaclustering") to reduce disk seeks during fsck and file deletion. Current ext3 places indirect blocks near data blocks scattered across disk. Dedicated reserved area pools indirect block allocations together. Andrew Morton questioned if benefits justify modifying stable filesystem - unplanned fsck during crisis is main use case, not planned downtime. No on-disk format change required.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/265813/][Ten-year timeline part 3: The Tucows years]]: LWN history April 2000 - January 2002 under Tucows. LinuxCare cancels IPO, layoffs. Red Hat dumps news site for venture capital. 2.4.0 finally released Jan 2001. Reiserfs merged for 2.4.1. Ramen worm, VA Linux layoffs. Eazel shuts down. Dmitry Sklyarov arrested under DMCA. Linus replaces 2.4.9 VM with new implementation. LWN unacquired Jan 31 2002, becomes independent again. tucows period overview of dotcom crash effects on Linux companies.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/266274/][A better ext4]]: ext4 merge plans for 2.6.25: lifts 4KB block size limit (up to 64KB, but must not exceed page size). 16-bit directory entry size field special-cased for 64KB. Block/group numbers become unsigned long; max filesystem size raised from 256PB. i_blocks field expanded to 48 bits for huge files; interpreted as filesystem blocks when >32bits. Journal checksums detect corrupt journals before recovery. i_version becomes unsigned 64-bit (NFSv4 change attribute). EXT4_IOC_MIGRATE ioctl converts inodes to ext4 format. Multi-block allocator enabled by default - complex heuristics for optimal block allocation groups. Test_fs mount flag proposed to require acknowledgment of developmental filesystem before mounting ext4.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/266320/][Memory management notifiers]]: Andrea Arcangeli's MMU notifier patch for KVM. Allows host to notify guests when pages swapped/reclaimed. Callbacks: release() when mm_struct going away, age_page() to query referenced bit, invalidate_page/range() when addresses no longer valid. Enables swap for KVM guests instead of pinning all memory. Also useful for GPUs with their own MMUs and RDMA engines. Shadow page tables currently prevent guest swap - host must pin pages or guest doesn't know when host reclaims memory.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/264402/][LWN.net: a ten-year timeline (part 1)]]: LWN history 1997-1999. Founded Jan 30 1998 by Jon Corbet and Forrest Cook after leaving NCAR. Original business plan: Red Hat support partner program for Eklektix Inc. First issue covered 2.1.79 kernel, devfs debate, Red Hat ADDL. Growth through dotcom era. Key events: Eric Raymond pushes "open source", Mozilla source release, Oracle ports to Linux, KDE/GNOME wars, Intel/Netscape invest in Red Hat, Red Hat IPO Aug 1999. IBM announces Linux support Feb 1999. Mindcraft study benchmark debates. BitKeeper licensing controversy. Training business plan failed; search for alternatives led to eventual acquisition offers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/266506/][Ten-year timeline part 4: the end and the beginning]]: LWN history 2002-2003. BitKeeper adopted for kernel development; proprietary SCM at core of Linux controversial. Martin Dalecki's IDE cleanup patches break systems. LWN announces shutdown July 2002, readers donated ~$35K to save it. Credit card merchant bank seized donation funds as fraud; PayPal kept lights on. October 2002: first subscriber-only weekly edition. Eldred v. Ashcroft copyright case argued. Subscription model kept LWN alive. Martin Dalecki quits; 115 IDE cleanup patches deleted from 2.5. SCO becomes active under Darl McBride.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/267672/][LCA: Bringing X into a two-handed world]]: Peter Hutterer's Multi-Pointer X (MPX) talk at LCA. X currently has single core keyboard/pointer - multiple devices lose identity in core events. XInput extension exists but 100% of apps don't use it. MPX creates master (virtual) and slave (physical) devices. Three-tier event delivery: XInput event, then core event from master, then core event from attached master. Allows multiple independent pointers, two windows receiving keyboard input simultaneously. Problems: enter/leave event nesting for buttons, grab conflicts between devices, window placement for multi-user. Future: multi-touch screens with pressure, elevation; requires rethinking app paradigms.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/267968/][Ticket spinlocks]]: Nick Piggin's ticket spinlock implementation for 2.6.25. Old spinlock: atomic decrement, unfair (first to decrement wins). New: 16-bit ticket+owner fields like bakery ticketing system; FIFO ordering ensures waiting longest gets lock first. Fixes 2x runtime difference between threads on 8-core Opteron. Big-ticket variant for >256 CPUs uses 16-bit fields (up to 65536 CPUs). Slight overhead vs old but negligible vs cache miss cost. X86 maintainers merged it.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/268407/][Ten-year timeline part 5: Not just SCO]]: LWN history 2002-2004. Key events: BitKeeper flames, sendmail trojan. Cryptographic code merged into kernel. SCO files $1B lawsuit vs IBM March 2003; claim grew to $3B. MS buys $10M Unix license from SCO. Novell claims it owns Unix. XFree86 license change leads to X.org fork. Fedora launches. 2.6.0 kernel released Dec 2003. SELinux merged. Munich decides on Linux. Microsoft/Novell deal later. SCO ultimately failed; community more resistant to legal attacks now.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/269558/][The state of Nouveau, part I]]: Nouveau open-source NVIDIA driver project by Stéphane Marchesin, Ben Skeggs. Aims for 2D/3D acceleration on NV04 through G80 cards. GPU architecture: channels (private FIFO, context, subchannels for objects). RAMIN instance memory contains objects. RAMHT hash table locates context objects. Command FIFO with method offsets. Multiple clients: X (DDX) and OpenGL (DRI). TTM (Tungsten Graphics) memory manager being developed for kernel. Modern GPUs need kernel-mode setting, memory management in kernel.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/270081/][KHB: Synthesis]]: Valerie Aurora's review of Alexia Massalin's 1987 PhD thesis. Synthesis OS: lock-free OS with runtime code synthesis. Quajects: objects with embedded executable code (self-modifying). Callbacks for queue_full/empty notify callers. Optimizations: compile-time templates + runtime specialization (e.g., skip multiply when B=1). Lock-free synchronization using 2-word compare-and-swap on 68030. Single-producer/consumer queues can run synchronization-free. Philosophy: avoid sync whenever possible, encode data in one/two words, use lock-free ops, defer remaining sync to server thread. Prefigures RCU design. Requires 68030-specific CAS; hard to implement on modern CPUs. Valuable for rethinking synchronization design.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/270091/][The dangers of weak random numbers]]: Amit Klein found weaknesses in OpenBSD's 1997 PRNG algorithm (used for DNS transaction IDs and IP fragmentation IDs). Required 6 minutes computation to predict IDs (vs seconds for BIND9). Enables cache poisoning, idle scanning, OS fingerprinting, blind data injection. BSDs (NetBSD, FreeBSD, DragonFly) adopted algorithm, changed it after Klein's research. Only OpenBSD/Mac OS X enable randomization by default. OpenBSD dismissive: "completely irrelevant in the real world." Linux's IP fragmentation ID RNG is cryptographically strong, but other PRNG weaknesses may exist in Linux apps.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/269562/][Reverse engineering: more than NVIDIA deserves?]]: Jonathan Corbet editorial on Nouveau ethics debate. Eight developers working free NVIDIA driver while Intel provides open drivers, AMD improving. Question: should community reward uncooperative companies by creating free drivers? Arguments: reverse engineering tradition; users with NVIDIA hardware need drivers; Intel/AMD don't deserve competitive disadvantage. Counter: companies providing docs/free drivers should be rewarded, not punished; creating Nouveau rewards NVIDIA's uncooperative stance. No consensus; individuals can't be prevented from working on any project.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/269276/][The state of Nouveau, part II]]: Part II covers Nouveau development status. TiNDC (Nouveau Development Companion) series tracks progress. Live glxgears demo at LCA 2007. X.org granted Vacation of Code for Arthur Huillet's Xv support. RandR1.2 improvements by Stuart Bennett, Maarten Maathuis. Current features: 2D acceleration (NV04-NV40), basic 3D for NV40+, Xv, DRI,电源 management. Still missing: HDMI, display scaling, TV out, proper power management, KMS. Collaboration with Intel's TTM memory manager development.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/271034/][Ten-year timeline part 6: almost to the present]]: LWN history 2004-2005. Key events: X.org launch, Fedora adds SELinux, SCO case ongoing. New kernel development model adopted (no 2.7 series). BitKeeper metadata locking controversy leads to git creation April 2005. Ubuntu announced (Sept 2004) and released (Oct 2004). Firefox 1.0, Solaris open-sourced. European software patent directive fight continues. -stable tree created due to quality concerns. Novel $500M anti-trust deal with Microsoft. Timeline ends with BitKeeper free license terminated, Linus creates git.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/270939/][Interoperating with Microsoft]]: Microsoft's February 2008 "expand interoperability" announcement: 30,000 pages protocol specs (Samba team had paid for these), API docs for Vista/.NET, SQL Server, Exchange. Patent enumeration for "reasonable and non-discriminatory" licensing - RAND terms unsuitable for GPL. Covenant not to sue (from Novell deal) doesn't protect developers implementing protocols. Office document format plug-in APIs promised. ODF vs OOXML considerations. Move from "Linux is a cancer" to "how open source influenced Windows Server 2008." Goal appears to be "Linux tax" - per-deployment payments. Actual impact small but acknowledges OSS role.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/270830/][The state of Nouveau, part 2]]: Part II details reverse engineering tools. renouveau: runs OpenGL tests against binary driver, dumps FIFO/register state changes for analysis. MMioTrace: traces kernel MMIO via ioremap replacement, uses page faults + single-step to record all register accesses (1MB+ logs). valgrind-mmt: traces X.org DDX FIFO from userspace. Example: YV12 video overlay reverse-engineered by comparing MMioTrace output between YUY2 and YV12 modes - found NV_PVIDEO.FORMAT bit 0 + 3 unknown registers. Gallium3D: new driver model separating state tracker, OS-dependent winsys, hardware-specific 3D driver. TTM memory manager adapted for NVIDIA with fence classing. Current status: 2D/EXA working all cards, Xv working (blitter/overlay/video texture depending on GPU), basic RandR1.2, Gallium3D driver for NV4x/NV5x exists but buggy. 3D: glxgears works on NV1x/NV3x/NV4x as proof-of-concept, no usable games yet.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/271762/][NDISwrapper dodges another bullet]]: NDISwrapper lets Windows wireless drivers run on Linux via GPL wrapper. January 2008 kernel change broke it by marking wrapper itself (not just kernel) as tainted, blocking GPL-only symbol access. Restored in 2.6.25-rc4 after backlash. Debate: GPL-only symbols as license enforcement vs. technical restriction. Linus: Windows drivers aren't derivative works of kernel, NDISwrapper not a GPL violation. NDISwrapper marks loaded drivers as tainted; problem is wrapper accessing GPL-only USB/workqueue symbols. Suggestion: clearer policy on GPL symbol exports. init_mm export issue (NVidia binary uses it) shows ongoing tension. Users with no open-source driver alternatives (broadcom wireless) depend on it.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/271817/][Realtime adaptive locks]]: Novell developers improve realtime kernel throughput. Problem: sleeping spinlocks in -rt patchset reduce throughput 10x (dbench: 1500MB/s normal -> 170MB/s rt). Solution: adaptive locks - spin briefly instead of sleeping immediately, since lock hold times short. Constraints: max 10000 spin iterations before sleeping, sleep if lock owner on same CPU, sleep if lock owner itself sleeping. Also allows lock stealing between equal-priority running processes to avoid context switches. Results: adaptive locks raise rt throughput to 700MB/s - still below mainline but much improved. hackbench improved significantly, kernel build slightly better.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/271614/][An object debugging infrastructure]]: Thomas Gleixner's debug_obj infrastructure to track kernel object lifetime bugs. Tracks objects via debug_obj_descr structure with fixup callbacks for init/activate/deactivate/destroy/free operations. Maintains hashed object lists, detects: deactivating inactive objects, reinitializing active objects, double-add, use-after-free. On error, logs backtrace at origin point (not crash site). Example: timer added via add_timer() but never removed before free - debug hook in kfree() catches still-active objects. Currently covers only timer subsystem; kobjects next. Can be configured out to avoid overhead. Helps find corrupted timer bombs before they cause crashes.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/272615/][Still waiting for Flash]]: Gnash 0.8.2 (first beta) tested. YouTube videos play but silent, skipping doesn't work, other sites fail. Google Flash charts blank, travel sites broken, games partially work. Flash ads mostly work. Conclusion: "lot of work yet to be done" - calling it beta is premature. Swfdec tested with worse results. Adobe's EULA forbids reverse engineering, restricts non-PC devices. Gnome/Flash incompatibility. Adobe Flash still buggy on Linux (crashes, 100% CPU). Flash is fact of life for interactive web; free alternatives still inadequate.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/272693/][A better DMA memory allocator]]: Andi Kleen's patch replaces old DMA zone with configurable mask-based allocator. Grabs memory from buddy allocator at boot, manages via bitmap instead of slab. API: alloc_pages_mask() with address mask parameter, get_pages_mask(), mempool_create_pool_pmask(). Tries normal allocation first, falls back to reserved pool if address constraint violated. Doesn't round to power-of-two (reduces fragmentation). Replaces GFP_DMA flag eventually. Eliminates separate swiotlb pool - unified DMA area. Devices with 30/31-bit limitations now handled properly. Old GFP_DMA users should switch to dma_set_mask() instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/273030/][How to use a terabyte of RAM]]: Daniel Phillips' Ramback patch. Concept: entire RAM-based disk with persistent backing store (Violin Memory). All I/O hits ramdisk (fast), kernel syncs to storage in background. fsync() doesn't block - backing store not consistent during normal operation. "Believe in your battery, Linux and the hardware." Eliminates disk I/O delays in steady-state. Controversial: data integrity concerns, no filesystem ordering guarantees. For mission-critical apps requiring consistency, traditional storage better. Useful for: embedded devices with RAM >> flash, non-critical data. Tested on Violin 500GB memory appliance with PCI-e interface.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/273731/][Generic semaphores]]: Matthew Wilcox's patch replaces 20+ architecture-specific semaphore implementations with single generic version (7600 lines -> 314). struct semaphore { spinlock_t lock, int count, list_head wait_list }. After 2.6.16 mutexes replaced most semaphores, hand-tuned arch code no longer needed. Semaphores still used where counting feature needed or lockdep can't handle pattern (tree structures with top-down/bottom-up locking). New features: down_killable(), down_timeout(). Completions could be rewritten using new semaphores.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/274008/][A new suspend/hibernate infrastructure]]: Rafael Wysocki's pm_ops structure with 20 device callbacks for suspend/hibernate. Suspend: prepare()->suspend()->resume()->complete(). Hibernate adds freeze()->poweroff() before image save, quiesce()->restore()->complete() on resume. Also thaw()/recover() for error recovery. Separate pm_noirq callbacks for interrupts-disabled/single-CPU state. Goal: fix suspend/hibernate reliability by separating the two cases. Concerns: complexity, may be overdesigned for simple hibernate.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/274859/][Striking gold in binutils]]: Ian Lance Taylor's "gold" linker - drop-in replacement for ld, up to 5x faster. Supports only x86/x86_64 ELF. Fresh start, focused design, C++ implementation (controversial). Only supports one object format unlike ld's many. Clean code, state machine implementation. Mozilla/KDE developers interested for faster linking. Next goal: concurrent linking (run compiler and linker simultaneously). Part of GNU toolchain ecosystem including quagmire (replacing autoconf/automake/libtool) and ggx (complete toolchain for custom CPU design).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/274755/][Kernel markers and binary-only modules]]: Mathieu Desnoyers' patch allows proprietary modules to contain kernel markers (static tracepoints merged in 2.6.24). Argument: markers help proprietary module authors debug/export info without exposing core kernel. Ingo Molnar opposed: binary modules should be isolated, marker API stability matters. Patch likely deferred - no markers defined in mainline yet, debate academic until actual usage. Marker API may change making binary compatibility tricky.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/274988/][Predictive ELF bitmaps]]: Andi Kleen's experimental patches prefetch pages based on training runs. Records faulted pages at exit, stores bitmap at end of ELF executable. Allows kernel to prefetch commonly-used pages on future runs, reducing disk seeks. Problems: bitmap storage awkward (end of ELF), single usage pattern system-wide. Alternatives: extended attributes (limited to 4K), linker-generated header (requires relink). Ulrich Drepper: should use userspace tools (systemtap) to collect data, not modify ELF. Andrew Morton: most gains come from eliminating seeks; solid-state disks will make this irrelevant.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/274863/][Introducing Sphinx]]: Sphinx Python documentation tool by Georg Brandl (BSD license). Created for Python docs, generates HTML/LaTeX/PDF from reStructuredText sources. Features: semantic markup, auto cross-references, hierarchical structure, auto-generated indexes, syntax highlighting via Pygments, extensible. LWN uses Sphinx for its site. Useful for hand-written documentation vs auto-generated API docs (use Epydoc for that). Cross-platform, templatable via Jinja.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/276039/][SDCC, the Small Device C Compiler]]: SDCC (Small Device C Compiler) by Sandeep Dutta - free GPL cross-compiler for 8051, 80DS390, Z80, 68HC08, PIC16/PIC18. Features: retargetable assembler/linker (ASXXXX/ASLINK), extensive MCU language extensions, global register allocator, loop invariants, constant folding, copy propagation, dead code elimination, switch jump tables. Version 2.8.0 adds PIC device support, universal Mac binaries, --acall-ajmp optimization, synchronized with GNU cpp 4.2.3. Produces Intel Hex files for target MCUs. Allows embedded apps in C for 8-bit microcontrollers (Z80, MCS51 still widely used). Alternative to proprietary Windows-based embedded C compilers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/275808/][Toward better direct I/O scalability]]: Nick Piggin's fast_gup() for lockless get_user_pages(). Problem: get_user_pages() requires mmap_sem lock, causing contention when multiple processes do direct I/O (common in large databases). Solution: use PAGE_BIT_SPECIAL flag in PTE to mark pages without struct page, enabling lockless operation for normal pages. fast_gup() attempts lockless page pin, falls back to slow get_user_pages() on failure. Result: 10% performance improvement running OLTP workload on 2-socket/8-core system with IBM DB2. Merged as get_user_pages_fast() in 2.6.27.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/276205/][Biometrics for identification]]: Bruce Schneier principle: "Biometrics are unique identifiers, but they are not secrets." CCC published German minister's fingerprint from photo, created film fake that fooled scanners. Fingerprints leave traces on everything touched - not secret. Spoofing most scanners possible with photo + live finger (body temperature/pulse). Once compromised, revocation impossible (you can't change your fingerprint). Other biometrics (voice, face, retina) susceptible to secret recording/photography. US collecting 10 fingerprints of all non-citizen visitors (99-year retention). Biometrics useful only with trusted path from reader to verifier; not suitable as secrets/keys. Unique identifier != secret.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/276059/][OOXML gets ISO approval]]: Microsoft's Office Open XML approved as ISO standard (April 2008) despite close vote. 24/32 P-members approved (needed 2/3), 86% O-members (needed 75%). Microsoft/Ecma addressed 3500 comments at 5-day BRM in Geneva but most not discussed - voted en masse. ISO fast-track process meant for "mature" standards - OOXML clearly not. Concerns: OOXML vs ODF competition (both ISO-approved now), MS won't implement ODF, proprietary lock-in via patent claims, "billions of documents" claim misleading (mostly legacy .doc not .docx). Tactics questioned (voting irregularities, "gaming" ISO). Standard still 6000+ pages. ODF advocates say ODF momentum continues regardless.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/276047/][WebKit rising]]: KDE's KHTML forked to WebKit (Apple's Safari engine). Konqueror moving to WebKit (KDE 4.0+ has QWebKit). GNOME's Epiphany browser also switching from Gecko to WebKit. WebKit designed for embedding (unlike Gecko), integrates well with Cairo/Pango. Safari ~3% browser share, Konqueror 4.5% - combined with Firefox 60%+ could challenge IE 15%. KDE/GNOME cooperation improving; WebKit has no copyright assignment requirement, reasonable licensing. Gecko controlled by Mozilla Corp with different agenda. WebKit seen as promising core Linux desktop component.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/277132/][Distribution-friendly projects Part 2]]: Diego Pettenò's guide for upstream developers. Technical needs: sane version info (major.minor.micro scheme), internal libraries cause problems (zlib example - security bugs require patching many copies), prefer --with-system-libfoo options. Avoid "automagic dependencies" - provide --disable-feature switches. Allow distribution control over CFLAGS/LDFLAGS, don't hardcode in configure.ac. Provide configure options to use system libraries instead of bundled copies. Source archives should match project-version.tar.gz format, same directory name inside archive.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/278137/][GCC and pointer overflows]]: CERT advisory warned GCC 4.2+ optimizes away pointer overflow checks (buffer + len >= buffer_end || buffer + len < buffer becomes just first test). C standard: pointer addition won't yield value outside same object, so overflow test always false. CERT said avoid GCC 4.2+ - but xlC, LLVM, PathScale, MSVC all do same optimization. Solution: check len >= BUFLEN instead of pointer arithmetic. GCC developers upset at unfair targeting. Code using pointer wrap detection is buggy per C standard. New warnings planned to alert programmers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/278016/][e1000 v. e1000e]]: Linux has two drivers for Intel e1000: e1000 (older PCI) and e1000e (newer PCI-Express). 2.6.26 plans to remove PCI-Express IDs from e1000 driver, forcing users to e1000e. Problem: Ingo Molnar built e1000 into kernel but e1000e as module - didn't load, network broken. Config trick added: e1000 won't claim PCIe device if e1000e built-in. Change will proceed but with better config handling to prevent others falling into trap.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/282038/][Debian, OpenSSL, and a lack of cooperation]]: Debian's OpenSSL 2006 patch crippled RNG for debugging (valgrind). Removed line using uninitialized memory for entropy - but that line was essential for cryptographic randomness. Debian hacker Kurt Roeckx asked on openssl-dev list, got little response, one developer said "I'm in favor of removing them." Keys generated 2006-2008 easily predictable. Problem: OpenSSL dev list not actually for development discussion; Debian didn't submit patch upstream; no clear upstream contact; coordination failure. Ben Laurie: should have submitted patch properly, openssl-dev not right venue. Security-critical packages need close upstream-downstream cooperation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/281938/][The big kernel lock strikes again]]: Generic semaphores (2.6.26) caused 40% AIM benchmark regression. Problem: new semaphore code too fair - thread at queue head gets lock only when running, causing 50% idle time. Solution: lock stealing - available semaphore goes to first requester regardless of queue order. BKL converted to spinlock temporarily. Linus: suboptimal BKL may finally motivate developers to remove it entirely. TTY code rework by Alan Cox still needed before full BKL removal.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/282250/][Getting a handle on caching]]: x86 PAT (Page Attribute Tables) support merged in 2.6.26. PAT bits in page table entries control caching per-page (UC, WC, WB). MTRRs limited (8 entries), PAT more flexible. ioremap() now always uncached; use ioremap_cache() for cached, ioremap_wc() for write-combining. set_memory_uc/wc/wb() functions available. UC minus = uncached unless MTRR says write-combining. PAT enables write-combining on memory-mapped video RAM without consuming MTRR entries.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/281965/][Extending system calls]]: Problem: pipe(), socket(), accept() can't set close-on-exec/non-blocking flags atomically. sys_indirect() proposed but deemed too complex/ugly. Linus preferred flag-overloading approach: socket(PF_INET, SOCK_STREAM|SOCK_CLOEXEC). New syscalls: paccept(), epoll_create2(), dup3(), pipe2(), inotify_init1(). Existing calls using overload trick: signalfd4(), eventfd2(), timerfd_create(), socket(), socketpair(). Goal for 2.6.27.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/283383/][Session cookies for web applications]]: Stateless session cookies store state in cookie itself with HMAC. Server stores secret key; cookie = data+expiry+HMAC(data, secret). Problem: if attacker gets secret (SQL injection), can forge cookies. Hardened stateless cookies: authenticator derived from password using iterative hashing (256 iterations of SHA-256 with salt). Attacker with database access still can't create valid cookies without breaking SHA-256. Protects against weak passwords being cracked offline.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/283103/][Appropriate sources of entropy]]: IRQF_SAMPLE_RANDOM from network drivers controversial - observable/manipulatable by attackers, and high traffic disables RX interrupts (reduces entropy). Proposal to remove from 12 remaining drivers. /dev/urandom adequate for most; /dev/random only for sensitive keys. Alternative: mix network data without entropy credit (dont_count_entropy flag). Embedded systems: sample temperature sensors, fan speed, smartctl output. Hardware RNGs (VIA Padlock) need user-space rngd daemon with FIPS tests before feeding kernel. virtio random number generator for guests.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/283066/][Kill BKL Vol. 2]]: BKL persists because it provides unique semantics: nests, allows sleeping, auto-releases on schedule(). Makes code assume uniprocessor. Ingo Molnar's kill-BKL tree: converts BKL to lockdep-tracked mutex, removes special schedule() behavior. push BKL acquisition down into driver open() functions first, then remove chrdev_open() BKL. 100+ drivers need explicit lock_kernel() in open() before BKL can be removed. Alan Cox: "empty_open() needs lock/unlock to serialize with initialization." Goal: make BKL visible/auditable via lockdep.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/283161/][Barriers and journaling filesystems]]: ext3/ext4 disable barriers by default due to 30% performance penalty. Barrier = forces disk to write all preceding blocks before writing following ones. Needed because drives reorder writes internally. Journal on contiguous blocks naturally ordered - commit record follows journal data, rarely reordered. But journal wrap-around breaks this. Chris Mason torture-test can trigger corruption without barriers. SUSE enables barriers by default. Solution: ext4 journal checksums eliminate first barrier; defer barriers when possible. LVM device-mapper doesn't pass barriers through. Battery-backed RAID controllers can safely disable barriers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/286244/][Peter Zijlstra: From DOS to kernel hacking]]: Interview with Peter Zijlstra tracing his path from DOS demo coder to Red Hat kernel hacker. Started Linux around Win95 era attracted by multitasking/memory protection, but didn't submit first kernel patches until ~10 years later after learning Unix system programming, lurking on LKML for 5 years, and encountering Rik van Riel's page replacement work. Current projects include group scheduling and -rt realtime work. Advice: just start hacking and don't be discouraged by criticism.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/287435/][Freezing filesystems and containers]]: Two freezing patches: Takashi Sato's filesystem freeze ioctl(FIFREEZE/FITHAW) extending XFS's freeze_bdev() to generic filesystem access for backup snapshots; and Matt Helsley's cgroup freezer using swsusp infrastructure to freeze all processes in a container (via cgroup filesystem freezer.state file) for future checkpoint/restart and migration. Pavel Machek ACK'd the patches.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/305782/][Hierarchical RCU]]: Paul McKenney's detailed explanation of Tree RCU, a redesign addressing Classic RCU's scalability limitations. Introduces a hierarchy of rcu_node structures to reduce global lock contention from O(n) to O(log n) CPUs per grace period—enabling support for 4096+ CPUs (up to ~256,000 on 64-bit). Also covers dynticks-idle CPU awareness to avoid waking sleeping CPUs, comprehensive rcutorture testing across 15 kernel config combinations, and the state-machine implementation for grace periods, quiescent states, and CPU hotplug handling.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/307732/][MinGW and why Linux users should care]]: Interview with Red Hat's Richard Jones and Dan Berrange on the Fedora MinGW project for cross-compiling Windows binaries from Linux. Enables open source projects (libvirt, Inkscape) to ship Windows builds without leaving Linux, breaking proprietary lock-in. Covers 50+ cross-compiled libraries, autoconf best practices, LGPL licensing decisions (vs Cygwin), and Gnulib for POSIX portability. Red Hat's motivation: growing libvirt's 10-20x potential user base and contributor pool.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/307482/][UKUUG: Arnd Bergmann on interconnecting with PCIe]]: Arnd Bergmann's talk on using PCIe as a low-latency, low-power interconnect for supercomputers instead of InfiniBand (saves 10-30W per node). Covers the Axon device, Roadrunner supercomputer's triblade (Opteron+Cell), virtual ethernet over non-transparent bridges via virtio abstraction, DMA virtqueue designs (MMIO vs DMA-based), and plans to replace custom ioctl() interface with InfiniBand verbs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/307713/][UKUUG: The right way to port Linux]]: Arnd Bergmann's talk on creating a generic "example" architecture (3000-line patch) to reduce code duplication in Linux ports. Problems addressed: copy-paste from x86, duplicated termbits.h, broken sys_ipc() implementations, incorrectly padded sembuf.h. Uses Open Firmware device tree, stubs out MMU/SMP/atomics, defines canonical ABI with asm-generic includes. Git tree at kernel.org/playground.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/308445/][Character devices in user space]]: Tejoun Heo's CUSE (Character devices in User Space) patch implementing character devices on top of FUSE (~1000 lines). Enables legacy driver support without kernel code, e.g., OSS proxy for /dev/dsp via userspace. Unlike UIO, CUSE handles no hardware or interrupts—everything goes through FUSE. Covers the ugly ioctl() memory access mechanism needed for unrestricted ioctls, poll() sleeping support added to FUSE, and potential for Palm Pilot sync, SunRay sound, network serial ports.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/308426/][Driver API: sleeping poll(), exclusive I/O memory, and DMA API debugging]]: Three driver API changes: (1) Tejoun He's poll() fix allowing sleepable poll callbacks via custom wakeup function (merge-bound for 2.6.29), (2) Arjan van de Ven's MMIO exclusivity API (pci_request_region_exclusive()) to prevent userspace mmap() of driver-allocated I/O memory, with iomem=relaxed boot param for dev systems, (3) Joerg Roedel's DMA API debugging facility to catch bugs like buffer size mismatches and direction confusion—already found bugs in networking drivers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/309298/][Debugfs and the making of a stable ABI]]: Discussion triggered by Remi Colinet's /proc/mempool proposal revealing that debugfs claims non-ABI status but effectively becomes ABI anyway since distros ship tools depending on it (e.g., usbmon). Matt Mackall warns moving things from /proc to debugfs just defers the problem. Linus's dictum: an ABI is defined by usefulness and availability, not documentation—only kernel-internal interfaces can truly avoid ABI formation. /proc/mempool still has no home.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/309400/][Variations on fair I/O schedulers]]: Goldwyn Rodrigues covering two fair I/O scheduler extensions: (1) Fabio Checconi/Paolo Valente's Budget Fair Queuing (BFQ) using sector budgets instead of time slices, B-WF2Q+ algorithm for O(log N) worst-case vs CFQ's O(N) jitter, self-learning budgets, hierarchical cgroup support with strict priority classes; (2) Satoshi Uchida's cfq-cgroups patch extending CFQ with hierarchical cgroup scheduling, per-device priorities via cfq.ioprio, time slice based on device priority. BFQ better at bandwidth distribution, cfq-cgroups offers more administrative control.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/310391/][A new realtime tree]]: New -rt tree restructuring spinlocks into lock_t type (mutex on RT, spinlock normally) via acquire_lock/release_lock API to eliminate macro trickery. Covers 20,000+ spin_lock calls needing conversion, scripts to automate it, threaded IRQ handlers, priority workqueues, and a run-time patching mechanism to switch between realtime and normal modes at boot. One of Linux's biggest out-of-tree forks after 4+ years finally organizing toward mainline inclusion.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/311630/][System calls and 64-bit architectures]]: preadv()/pwritev() proposal reveals complexity of passing 64-bit args on 32/64-bit architectures with different calling conventions (MIPS/PARISC need aligned register pairs). Solution: split offset into two 32-bit args for compat layer, use merge_64() for MIPS endian handling. VFS implementation trivial; Christoph Hellwig found a bug in existing compat_sys_readv/writev causing incorrect accounting. BSD-compatible user interface maintained but internal syscall ABI differs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/289137/][Multiqueue networking]]: David Miller's multiqueue transmit patch introducing netdev_queue structure per transmit queue with own lock. alloc_etherdev_mq() replaces alloc_etherdev(), skb_get_queue_mapping() picks queue, select_queue() callback for WME devices. Drivers for single-queue devices unchanged; multiqueue support adds netif_tx_start/wake/stop_all_queues() helpers. Aims for 2.6.28 merge, groundwork in 2.6.27. Benefits virtualization by pushing tx lock into per-queue locks.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/290227/][Handling kernel security problems]]: Debate triggered by PaX Team/grsecurity over kernel not explicitly marking security fixes in commits/announcements. Linus: commit is disclosure, explicit marking is "security theater", all bugs have security implications. Stable releases lack CVE numbers, "STRONGLY encouraged" is the hint. Distributors track security fixes for their kernels; grsecurity seen as potentially benefiting from raising Linux security issue profile. Key tension: grep-friendly commit messages help attackers vs. opaque messages merely slowing them.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/292081/][OLS: SELinux from academia to your desktop]]: James Morris's OLS talk tracing SELinux from NSA research through MAC adoption, rejection at 2001 Kernel Summit leading to LSM, Fedora FC2's "targeted" policy, Tresys's reference policy replacing spaghetti code, loadable policy modules, setroubleshoot GUI, RCU for performance, usability improvements, expansion beyond kernel into XSM, XACE, labeled NFS, SEPostgreSQL. Challenges: usability, documentation.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/291826/][The lockless page cache]]: Nick Piggin's lockless page cache patches for 2.6.27. Introduces get_user_pages_fast() without mmap_sem, page_cache_get_speculative() using atomic_cmpxchg() so lookups don't need mapping semaphore—reference counts checked speculatively. Removes core locks from page cache, improves multi-core scalability (~10% on threaded database workload, helps even single-threaded perf).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/291896/][OLS: The state of Linux wireless networking]]: John Linville's OLS talk covering Linux wireless history: Full MAC (firmware-enforced) vs Soft MAC (ieee80211/SoftMAC mistake), Devicescape stack → mac80211 merged 2.6.22, libertas OLPC as exception (Full MAC with special power-saving needs). compat-wireless backports to 2.6.21. Future: cfg80211 replacing wireless extensions, AP mode, mesh networking. Issues: firmware licensing, vendor participation (Broadcom the outlier), regulatory compliance.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/293037/][Kernel Hacker's Bookshelf: The Practice of Programming]]: Val Aurora's review of Kernighan & Pike's book on simplicity/clarity/generality. Performance chapter: don't optimize until measurable reason, profiling, spam-filter optimization case study. Notation chapter: little languages, printf format specs, ~100-line grep, Awk/Tcl/Perl scripts, Z-machine virtual machine. Argues for 2x productivity gain over 10x payoff.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/293575/][Kernel-based checkpoint and restart]]: Dave Hansen/Oren Laadan's checkpoint/restart syscall API (checkpoint(pid, fd, flags), restart(crid, fd, flags)). Requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN, currently checkpoints VM state, processor state, task struct only—"hello world" level. Containers are prerequisite since single processes have too many relations. Related: OpenVZ, BLCR (Berkeley Lab), cryopid, Condor, Kerrighed. Two syscalls only but kernel involvement required for file descriptors, sockets, timers, epoll, ptrace, CPU affinity, futexes, SELinux state.
