- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/293689/][Udev rules and the management of the plumbing layer]]: Kay Sievers pushes for unified udev rules across distros (moving from /etc to /lib), calling rules "code not config". Marco d'Itri (Debian) refuses, claims his rules are more "elegant". Scott Remnant (Ubuntu) has technical concerns about initramfs. DeviceKit replacing HAL. David Zeuthen proposes requiring kernel subsystems to provide udev rules before mainline merge. Linux Plumbers Conference spawned from these plumbing layer issues.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/294465/][Triggers: less busy busy-waiting]]: Jeremy Fitzhardinge's trigger API for atomic-context waiting (trigger_reset/wait/finish/kick). Generic implementation uses cpu_relax() busy-wait; x86 uses monitor/mwait instructions to halt processor until variable changes—真正的省电. Paravirt_ops allows hypervisor to implement triggers by suspending VM entirely. Elegant but Arjan worried about mwait latency.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/294675/][Regulating wireless devices]]: Luis Rodriguez's CRDA (Central Regulatory Domain Agent) introduces struct ieee80211_regdomain per country with frequency ranges, bandwidth, power, antenna gain limits. Signed binary database in userspace, crda utility queries via netlink, notifies drivers via notifier. Prevents illegal power/frequency use. mac80211 queries regulatory domain from AP hints or EEPROM. Ham radio users could potentially unlock more frequencies. Some FCC-like agencies doubt open-source regulatory compliance.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/294642/][Tangled up in threads]]: MAP_32BIT flag forces thread stacks into bottom 2GB for historical context-switch optimization on early 64-bit CPUs. Causes linear VMA search as thread count grows—100 threads/sec creation rate instead of normal. Modern CPUs no longer need this optimization. Solution: new MAP_STACK flag (currently ignored, for future kernel use), glibc will switch. ABI concerns prevent removing MAP_32BIT. OCaml compiler relies on MAP_32BIT for a bug workaround.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/296444/][DRI, BSD, and Linux]]: Dave Airlie's DRM development process overhaul—DRM tree to rebase on mainline kernel, standard mailing list patch review, no releases depending on non-upstream DRM features. Problem: shared code directory benefits BSD (10-15 Linux devs vs 1 FreeBSD + 1 OpenBSD). FreeBSD's Robert Noland may lose ability to veto imports breaking BSD; GPL developer refused because DRM won't take GPL code. Shared-core tree never really worked—each OS shipped its own port anyway.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/296738/][Kernel Hacker's Bookshelf: UNIX Internals]]: Val Aurora reviews Uresh Vahalia's 1996 book. KMA chapter covers 8 allocators: resource map, power-of-two lists, McKusick-Karels, buddy, lazy buddy, Mach zone, Dynix hierarchical (Paul McKenney), SLAB. File systems: s5fs problems (metadata all in one place), FFS distribution of inodes. SLAB still best-of-breed; second edition cancelled. File systems coverage dated (no COW, extents, btrees). Bookshelf series inspired by seeing it on every serious kernel hacker's shelf.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/296578/][High- (but not too high-) resolution timeouts]]: Arjan van de Ven's schedule_hrtimeout() enables high-res timers for poll/select. Current: ms-resolution jiffies (up to 10ms error). Solution: hrtimer-based with fuzz proportional to timeout length (10ns-100ms accuracy range). New hrtimer accessor API, soft/hard expiration ranges, timer_slack_ns per task (prctl, default 50μs). RT tasks get 0 slack. Timeouts >10s use 100ms accuracy minimum.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/296419/][SCHED_FIFO and realtime throttling]]: 2.6.25 introduced rt_bandwidth limiting default group to 0.95/1.0s (5% reserved). Peter Zijlstra wanted "unlimited" default; Ingo Molnar prefers raising to 10s since runaway SCHED_FIFO blocking admin access is a bug. Nick Piggin warned: some apps engineer 90% CPU + 10% margin, throttle could hit in production not testing. Linus: kernel has plenty of policy, defaults should match what distros set. Debate over API compliance vs safety. SCHED_RR doesn't solve it—same problem within priority level.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/297500/][System calls and rootkits]]: Arjan van de Ven's syscall entry sanity check (verifies call address in kernel code region, ~2 cycle overhead). Andi Kleen: rootkits just hook entry point first instruction instead. Alan Cox: only hypervisor can provide irrevocable read-only pages for guests. Ingo Molnar proposed randomized compiler-inserted rootkit checks (high cost, complex). Real solution: hardware enforcement (TPM) or locked-down virtualization. Without it, Linux defenseless against root as root. Patch abandoned as "exercise in measuring cost."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/297954/][Review: Intellectual Property and Open Source]]: O'Reilly book by Van Lindberg. Code analogies for lawyers: patents like regex, contracts like distributed VCS, trademarks like desktop icons. Covers copyright, patents, trademarks, trade secrets, contracts. GPL called "Schrödinger's license" (predates JMRI appeals court). Chapter on accepting patches recommends signed contributor agreements; incorrectly claims BusyBox needed only two copyright holders (actually many). Notable omissions: software patents almost absent, Microsoft/Novell deal unmentioned. US law only. Valuable for beginners despite flaws.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/298758/][User manuals for free software]]: FLOSS Manuals project creating collaborative user documentation using book sprints and wiki-style editing. Produces professional HTML/PDF/dead-tree manuals. Supports "remix" feature—custom book creation from chapters. Covers OLPC XO Sugar manuals, Firefox, OpenOffice, WordPress. Mirrors free software development model. 80+ manuals available. Aims to make free software accessible to average users.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/299483/][LPC: Booting Linux in five seconds]]: Arjan van de Ven/Auke Kok (Intel) demo 5-second boot on EEE PC. "Done means CPU and disk idle"—no splash screen cheating. Budget: kernel 1s, early boot 1s, X 1s, desktop 2s. Kernel: no initrd, all boot modules built-in, async AHCI/UHCI init, readahead. Userspace: back to plain init, sReadahead parallel with fsck→D-Bus→X→desktop, HAL/udev/networking after. 75MB max reads at 25MB/s Flash speed. "Don't settle for make boot faster—make boot fast."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/300202/][e1000e and the joy of development kernels]]: e1000e driver in 2.6.27 corrupts EEPROM/NVM permanently, bricking adapters. Not reproducible, requires hardware replacement. GEM/PAT ruled out; correlation with Intel graphics. X.org hypothesis—driver mapping MMIO wrongly causing DMA写到EEPROM. Mandriva advisory, distributors pulling affected kernels. Mitigation: barrier patch merged in -rc8. Recovery tool difficult; Dave Airlie bricked his laptop trying. Lesson: development kernels can destroy hardware, not just corrupt filesystems.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/300066/][LPC: The future of Linux graphics]]: Keith Packard microconference. TTM abandoned in favor of GEM (Graphics Execution Manager) for memory management, being prepared for merge. Kernel-based mode setting: kernel controls video hardware, enables suspend/resume, non-root X, panic messages on display, fast user switching. Flag day concern: new API requires X server updates. Linus unhappy about big changes but accepted plan—GEM goes first, mode setting follows, both old/new X supported temporarily. 
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/300070/][Newer kernels and older SELinux policies]]: 2.6.25 made /proc/net a symlink to /proc/self/net for containers; tripped up SELinux policy checks on the symlink itself. Andrew Morton hit "Permission denied" on FC5/FC6. Distribution policies updated but older distros affected. Stephen Smalley: compatibility switches used for SELinux changes but other kernel changes can alter permission checks too. Eric Biederman proposes xattrs for /proc to label it properly. Gray area: SELinux policy interface vs. kernel ABI.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/301251/][The state of the e1000e bug]]: Update on EEPROM corruption bug. GEM and PAT cleared as suspects; strong correlation with Intel graphics (but NVIDIA report exists too). Race condition makes it hard to reproduce. Distributors disabled e1000e. Arjan van de Ven posted mitigation barrier patch, merged in -rc8—prevents NVM corruption but doesn't fix root cause. Recovery tool slow coming; Intel under pressure. Bisection risk: bug present in history even after fix. 2.6.27 release couldn't be delayed further. Ultimately traced to ftrace + module removal interaction, fixed properly later.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/300992/][Low-level tracing plumbing]]: Steven Rostedt's unified trace buffer (10th iteration)—per-CPU lockless circular buffers, compact event format (type:2, len:3, time_delta:27 in 32-bit header). 27-bit time delta for between-event times, 64-bit stamps only on overflow via RINGBUF_TYPE_TIME_EXTENT. relayfs criticized by Peter Zijlstra as "bad performing mess"—may be replaced. Goals: enable ftrace, LTTng, SystemTap to share buffer, allow cat/grep access. Struct page overloaded to track buffer pages. Dynamic tracing, static tracepoints, user-space tracing still unsolved.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/301135/][Plugging into GCC]]: GCC plugin API proposal—FSF feared proprietary plugins would abuse binary-only modules. Solution: modify libgcc runtime exception—if proprietary plugin generates code linked with libgcc/libstdc++, resulting executable must be GPL. Ian Lance Taylor: plugin API still unstable, Python/Perl scripting could force source distribution. Actual approach: plugin=derived work trigger for runtime exception removal. Timeline unclear. Users: EDoc++, Mozilla Dehydra, LLVM, MELT.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/302159/][Accessibility in Linux systems]]: Samuel Thibault's overview of Linux accessibility. CLI: speakup (speech), brltty (Braille) have worked for decades. GUI: AT-SPI (GNOME) + Orca screen reader now functional for GTK, Java/Swing, Mozilla, OpenOffice. Qt/KDE support coming via D-BUS rebase. Debian installer auto-detects Braille, installs brltty—complete independence for disabled users. Kernel basic Braille screen reader recently added. Future: bootloader accessibility, EFI speech support, better audio/motor accessibility tools.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/302251/][Btrfs to the mainline?]]: Chris Mason proposes merging Btrfs into mainline early for more review. Andrew Morton supports: "get it into linux-next asap, merge 2.6.29". Btrfs is copy-on-write, snapshot filesystem; disk format stabilization target end of 2008. Adrian Bunk skeptical—ext4 missed schedule by a year due to ext3 ghost. Mason argues Btrfs is different—more excitement, people want to test it. I/O errors and running out of space handling still rough. Separate code review from ext4 development easier in mainline.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/302043/][Moving interrupts to threads]]: Thomas Gleixner's threaded interrupt handlers—reduces interrupt-disabled time, simplifies locking between hard/soft IRQ handling. request_threaded_irq() replaces request_irq() with separate quick_check_handler (just ACKs interrupt, wakes thread) and full handler thread. Allows renicing interrupt threads, RT priority, cgroups. struct task_struct gains pointer to irqaction. May enable tasklet removal eventually. Andi Kleen worried about encouraging badly written interrupt code. 2.6.29 target.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/303561/][HTTP response splitting]]: HTTP response splitting (HRS) attack—inject CRLF (%0d%0a) into HTTP headers via user input to inject headers or body. Used for XSS, web cache poisoning, site hijacking. Old attack (Amit Klein 2004) but still widespread (Jeremiah Grossman found many sites vulnerable). Mono, Safari had recent HRS bugs. Filter CR/LF in user-controlled header values.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/324989/][Nftables: a new packet filtering engine]]: Patrick McHardy's nftables proposal to replace iptables with a virtual machine-based packet filtering system. Uses bytecode compiled from high-level rules, eliminating protocol-specific code duplication in kernel. Features include set lookups for multi-branch rules, verdict register for multiple actions per packet, and netlink-based API allowing individual rule modification. Controversial due to breaking existing iptables configurations but promises better optimization, validation, and long-term flexibility.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/313679/][BleachBit: Does GNU/Linux need the equivalent of a Windows registry cleaner?]]: Analysis of BleachBit utility for cleaning Linux home directory clutter (cache files, configuration remnants). Debate over necessity: minimal performance benefits (unused config files don't impact speed) vs privacy protection and disk space recovery. Critiques interface design (lacks sufficient warnings, selections persist between sessions) and questions whether distributions should enforce better package cleanup policies rather than relying on third-party tools.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/313682/][Btrfs aims for the mainline]]: Chris Mason's Btrfs filesystem proposal for 2.6.29 kernel merge, featuring copy-on-write design, snapshots, and integrated volume management. Key debates: user-space API stability, custom spinning mutex implementation (512 spin attempts before sleeping for hot cache performance), and naming convention (btrfs vs btrfsdev to indicate experimental status). Performance tests showed 30-50% speedup with custom locking on dbench workloads.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/314997/][SSL certificates and MD5 collisions]]: Analysis of SSL certificate vulnerability exploiting MD5 hash collisions to create forged certificates. RapidSSL's use of MD5 signatures and sequential serial numbers enabled researchers to generate colliding certificates with a PS3 cluster (4 attempts needed). CAs quickly switched to SHA-1 and randomized serial numbers. Discussion covers certificate chain vulnerabilities, browser trust models, and the cryptographic research progression from theoretical weaknesses to practical attacks.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/313437/][New NFS to bring parallel storage to the masses]]: NFSv4.1 specification with Parallel NFS (pNFS) enabling direct client access to storage devices, bypassing single-server bottlenecks. Uses metadata server (MDS) to provide data layout maps, allowing clients to stripe data across multiple storage devices. Supports file, block, and object storage types. Implementation planned for 2.6.30 (basic session management) and 2.6.31 (I/O operations). Benefits: higher I/O throughput, standardized parallel filesystem access vs vendor-specific solutions like Lustre/PanFS.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/314808/][An asynchronous function call infrastructure]]: Arjan van de Ven's async API for 2.6.29 kernel enabling parallel device initialization during boot. Uses async_schedule()/async_synchronize_cookie() functions to manage parallel execution while maintaining device registration order. Initial implementation targeted SCSI/libata subsystems but caused boot failures, requiring fastboot kernel parameter for activation. Internal implementation uses thread pools (max 256 threads) with pending/running function lists.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/314848/][Who is the best inliner of all?]]: Debate over GCC's CONFIG_OPTIMIZE_INLINING option allowing compiler to override inline keyword decisions. Linus opposed automatic inlining (arguing it harms debugging reliability), while Ingo Molnar's tests showed 1-7% smaller kernels with compiler-driven inlining. Core conflict: human control vs compiler optimization, with 30,000+ inline functions in kernel representing a "very strong habit" among developers.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/316274/][LCA: Catching up with X.org]]: Summary of X.org developments at Linux.conf.au 2009, focusing on Graphics Execution Manager (GEM) memory management and Kernel Mode Setting (KMS). GEM provides persistent GPU-accessible objects with global names, replacing texture dumping at task switches. KMS moves display control to kernel, enabling unprivileged X server, panic messages on display, and fast user switching. Both merged in 2.6.28/2.6.29 kernels respectively, representing major X modernization after years of stagnation.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/316206/][Book Review: Hacking VoIP]]: Review of Himanshu Dwivedi's "Hacking VoIP" covering security vulnerabilities in SIP, H.323, and IAX protocols. Analysis reveals weaknesses: MD5-based authentication vulnerable to dictionary attacks, RTP eavesdropping due to unencrypted audio streams, denial-of-service via packet flooding. Covers countermeasures like TLS, SRTP, and ZRTP encryption. Assessment shows most VoIP products (including open source like Asterisk/Ekiga) lack robust security implementation despite known vulnerabilities.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/317787/][Online defragmentation for ext4]]: Akira Fujita's patch for ext4 online defragmentation using EXT4_IOC_DEFRAG ioctl. User-space daemon creates temporary file, allocates contiguous blocks via fallocate(), then uses kernel ioctl to swap extents atomically. Addresses fragmentation without file movement or data loss, handling active files and directory entries. Comparison with XFS's xfs_fsr shows different approaches: XFS does user-space direct I/O but can't defrag active files, while ext4 kernel-based approach handles active files but is more complex.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/317814/][Taming the OOM killer]]: Analysis of out-of-memory killer improvements including cgroup-based oom.priority control and user-space notification proposals. Explains OOM killer heuristics: badness score based on memory usage, CPU time, process age, and oom_adj value (-17 to +15). Covers debates: name-based victim selection vs cgroup control groups, user-space memory freeing vs kernel killing. Highlights Android's lowmemory driver with multiple thresholds and embedded system considerations.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/318699/][How patches get into the mainline]]: Analysis of Linux kernel patch flow using git merge tracking for 2.6.29 cycle. Shows 107 trees feeding directly into mainline with shallow hierarchy (only one tree uses two intermediate layers). Statistics: 975/11,260 changesets go directly to Linus (mostly via Andrew Morton), David Miller's networking trees contribute most (1910), linux-2.6-tip (Ingo Molnar) contributes 1270. Notes limitations: git fast-forward merges lose repository origin information, cherry-picking obscures patch provenance.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/321663/][Interrupts, threads, and lockdep]]: Discussion of interrupt handling conflicts between lockdep's IRQF_DISABLED enforcement and i2c drivers requiring interruptible handlers. Lockdep assumes all interrupts run disabled, but i2c devices need slow register access requiring interruptible execution. Solution: Thomas Gleixner's threaded interrupt handler patch from realtime tree, allowing quick handlers to acknowledge interrupts and regular handlers to run in process context. Highlights tension between lock validation correctness and hardware access realities.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/319544/][Interview: the return of the realtime preemption tree]]: Thomas Gleixner and Ingo Molnar interview on renewed realtime preemption work after 1.5-year x86 maintenance sabbatical. Integration into linux-2.6-tip repository, focus on patch refactoring and mainline merging. Discussion of production readiness (2.6.24/26 versions), BKL removal efforts, and development philosophy: deterministic execution as "last major technological niche" not handled by stock kernel.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/319567/][Book Review: Nmap Network Scanning]]: Review of Gordon Lyon's comprehensive Nmap guide covering network scanning fundamentals, port scanning techniques, service/OS detection, and Nmap Scripting Engine (NSE). Analysis of security applications: firewall evasion, IDS bypassing, and defensive measures. Highlights book's technical depth, real-world examples, and educational value for both novice and experienced users.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/319686/][Getting the measure of ksize()]]: Discussion of ksize() function controversy - returns actual allocated size for kmalloc() objects but causes SLOB allocator issues. Debate over kernel export: useful for crypto data wiping and network packet buffer optimization vs risk of misuse on non-kmalloc objects. Highlights SLAB/SLUB vs SLOB allocator differences and memory allocation implementation details.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/319860/][From wakelocks to a real solution]]: Analysis of Android wakelock power management debate and alternatives. Wakelocks prevent system suspend but conflict with kernel design principles. Proposed solutions: inhibit_suspend()/uninhibit_suspend() functions, user-space daemon for policy decisions. Discussion of embedded vs x86 power management needs and application behavior protection arguments.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/322668/][A superficial introduction to fsblock]]: Nick Piggin's fsblock proposal to replace buffer_head structures with smaller fsblock structures supporting variable block sizes (including superpages up to 64KB). Features include metadata blocks for filesystem operations, reference counting, and improved memory efficiency. Aims to modernize kernel's block I/O layer while maintaining compatibility with existing filesystems. Currently in development since 2007, with potential for significant performance improvements on ext2 filesystem.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/322823/][ext4 and data loss]]: Analysis of ext4 delayed allocation feature causing unexpected data loss compared to ext3's implicit 5-second durability guarantee. Ext3's data=ordered mode forces data writes before metadata commits for security, while ext4's delayed allocation allows up to 60-second write delays. Discussion covers POSIX compliance debates, application developer expectations, and proposed solutions including forced allocation on file close/rename and user-space fsync() usage.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/322777/][Linux and 4K disk sectors]]: Industry transition from 512-byte to 4096-byte disk sectors to improve format efficiency and error correction at higher areal densities. Covers three-phase transition plan: 512B logical/512B physical → 512B logical/4KB physical (read-modify-write emulation) → 4KB logical/4KB physical. Discusses partitioning alignment issues, ATA-8 standard support, and filesystem implications for large block sizes.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/323752/][Better than POSIX?]]: Philosophical discussion on whether Linux should provide filesystem guarantees beyond POSIX requirements, focusing on ext4 delayed allocation controversy. Examines "worse is better" Unix philosophy vs. kernel-level reliability improvements. Argues for "good enough" robustness by default with stronger guarantees available via explicit coding, balancing performance with user expectations established by ext3 behavior.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/324028/][Developments in the GCC world]]: Overview of GCC 4.4 release delays due to runtime library exemption changes for plugin API licensing. Covers split stacks feature for dynamic thread stack allocation, link-time optimization (LTO) with GIMPLE serialization, and plugin API development integrating tools like Mozilla's Dehydra and MELT. Discusses FSF's GPL-only plugin restrictions and bytecode language classification debates.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/325369/][Shortening the rope]]: Analysis of Unix philosophy changes as Linux matures, focusing on X.org disabling Ctrl-Alt-Backspace X server kill and Ubuntu disabling "rm -rf /" by default. Examines balance between user control and safety, with debates over whether removing "surprising booby traps" contradicts Unix's "enough rope to hang yourself" philosophy. Covers alternative safety mechanisms and user customization options.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/327141/][Shortening the rope]]: Follow-up discussion on Unix safety changes, examining the controversy around X.org's Ctrl-Alt-Backspace default change and Ubuntu/Fedora disabling "rm -rf /". Debates whether removing dangerous defaults contradicts Unix philosophy or represents necessary safety improvements for broader user base. Covers Emacs user conspiracy theories, alternative safety approaches, and technical arguments about keyboard shortcuts and file deletion safeguards.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/327499/][Book Review: Pragmatic Version Control Using Git]]: Review of Travis Swicegood's Git book, covering installation, daily Git usage, branch management, and repository administration. Critiques the book's strengths as a practical guide for beginners while noting weaknesses in coverage of migration tools and server setup. Discusses Git terminology debates (index vs staging area) and the book's value as a quick reference despite rapid Git development pace.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/328363/][Rebasing and merging: some git best practices]]: Linus Torvalds' Git workflow guidelines for kernel development, emphasizing when to use rebase vs merge operations. Key rules: never rebase trees with public history, keep rebase-capable trees private, and minimize upstream merges to avoid introducing untested changes. Explains tradeoffs between clean history and testability, with recommendations for stable merge points.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/328436/][Solving the ext3 latency problem]]: Analysis of ext3 filesystem performance fixes for fsync() latency issues that caused Firefox freezes. Covers changes to I/O scheduling priorities, new WRITE_SYNC_PLUG operations, and controversial switch to data=writeback default mode. Examines security tradeoffs between performance and data integrity after system crashes.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/328487/][Alternatives to SQL Databases]]: Survey of non-SQL database options including Cassandra (Facebook's distributed store), Memcached (in-memory caching), Tokyo Cabinet (persistent key-value), Redis (disk-backed with advanced data types), CouchDB (JSON document database), and MongoDB (binary JSON format). Compares their tradeoffs between ACID properties, scalability, and data structure complexity for different application needs.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/305083/][Squashfs submitted for the mainline]]: Squashfs compressed read-only filesystem (used in Live CDs and embedded devices) submitted for mainline inclusion in 2.6.29 by Phillip Lougher; completely rewritten since previous attempt, now little-endian for cross-architecture compatibility, uses gzip compression achieving ~1/3 size of ext3; superior to cramfs with larger files/filesystems support, hard links, real inode numbers, and NFS exportability; Andrew Morton requested it be moved forward for linux-next inclusion.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/302854/][Connecting to Microsoft Exchange with OpenChange]]: OpenChange project provides MAPI-compatible API to connect Linux clients (Kontact, Evolution) to Microsoft Exchange server; offers email, shared folders, calendars, contacts via mapiprofile and openchangeclient command-line tools; Evolution integration exists but unstable; KDE 4.3 targeted as "sweet spot" for production use via akonadi; project seeks more developers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329223/][Oracle: SELECT * FROM Sun]]: Analysis of Oracle's April 2009 acquisition of Sun Microsystems and its implications for open source; covers MySQL (Oracle already owned InnoDB via Innobase, may broaden database product line), Solaris (likely maintained as legacy platform rather than Linux competitor), Btrfs (Oracle claims commitment unchanged), Java (called "most important software acquired"), and OpenOffice (orphan fit, hoped Oracle would open it to community). Author cautiously optimistic given Oracle's Linux investment and community-oriented posture.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329626/][A look at the MySQL forks]]: Overview of MySQL ecosystem forks that emerged after Oracle/Sun acquisition: Percona (XtraDB drop-in InnoDB replacement, SMP optimization), OurDelta (builds on Percona, adds Sphinx engine and monitoring tools), MariaDB (Monty Widenius's fork focusing on Maria crash-safe storage engine), and Drizzle (radical refactor targeting web/cloud infrastructure, no SQL compatibility). Notes InnoDB copyright now owned by Oracle after 2005 Innobase acquisition; community pre-emptively created alternatives.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329428/][In search of the perfect changelog]]: Kernel developers debate changelog quality and "Impact:" tags introduced by Ingo Molnar's patch submission style. Ingo claims Impact tags force smaller patches and help maintainers assess risk; Linus and others call them redundant and unhelpful. Jonathan Corbet proposes better changelog documentation covering audience (maintainers, distributors, bug hunters, users) and content (motivation, fix citations, API changes, follow-up notices). Subtext: most developers lack writing skills for clear changelogs.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329464/][The slow work mechanism]]: David Howells's slow work thread pool merged for 2.6.30 — a batch job facility for long-running kernel tasks (contrasted with workqueues for quick per-CPU jobs and async function calls for ordered tasks). Uses struct slow_work with get_ref/put_ref/execute ops; supports "very slow work" prioritized up to 50% of thread pool. Only user in 2.6.30 is FS-Cache; tunable via /proc/sys/kernel/slow-work. Author notes kernel has multiple thread pool implementations that could be consolidated.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329556/][Debian GNU/kFreeBSD: one more step towards a universal operating system]]: Debian port using FreeBSD kernel with GNU userland (glibc, dpkg/apt) — became official Debian architecture in April 2009. Advantages over Debian/Linux: FreeBSD's pf firewall, jails (container virtualization), ZFS support (CDDL licensed, avoids Linux licensing issues), cleaner kernel interfaces, Windows NDIS drivers in-tree. Disadvantages: smaller driver set, rougher installer (temporary sysinstall, debian-installer port planned for GSOC 2009). Currently ~80% of 7800 Debian source packages ported; ~5-8 core developers, ~50 daily users. Long-term vision is "Debian GNU/Any" — glibc as kernel abstraction layer for binary compatibility across any supported kernel.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/330866/][Linux ASLR vulnerabilities]]: Two bypass techniques for Linux ASLR disclosed at CanSecWest and Black Hat Europe 2009. (1) /proc/pid/stat and /proc/pid/wchan leak enough info (instruction pointers, wait channels) to deduce layout of a target process via repeated sampling. (2) ASLR's get_random_int() uses flawed RNG that only re-keys every 5 minutes (not every second as comments claim); attacker can predict random addresses for ~2 minutes by knowing pid + jiffies sum. Jake Edge submitted patches to restrict /proc access but no mainline response yet. Also notes security community frustration with kernel devs misclassifying exploitable flaws as "denial of service."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/329458/][ELC2009: Visualizing memory usage with smem]]: Matt Mackall's smem tool presented at Embedded Linux Conference — provides PSS (proportional set size, fair share of shared pages) and USS (unique set size) metrics via /proc/pagemap to solve Linux's "accounting problem" where ps/top count shared pages multiple times. Python tool with pie/bar chart visualizations. Can capture /proc data as tar for offline analysis, system-wide memory sizing output showing required RAM for given workload. Linus originally called pagemap "crap" but it was merged; all user-space tools broke and no one else built alternatives.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/331818/][The return of devfs]]: Proposal for devtmpfs — kernel-managed tmpfs mounted at /dev early in boot, populated automatically as devices register with driver core. ~300 lines of code, aims to eliminate udev coldplug delay (1-2 seconds that blocks all userspace), enable parallel boot, and help embedded/ rescue scenarios. Reminiscent of old devfs but "done right" per Kay Sievers: no lookup hooks, no modprobe behind back, no new naming scheme. Faces resistance: Andrew Morton amused ("Lol, devfs"), Alan Cox objects to kernel bloat and papering over userspace problems, Christoph Hellwig sees it as devfs rebranded. Goal: merge in 2.6.31.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/332602/][Random numbers for ASLR]]: Follow-up to ASLR RNG flaw (from article 330866). Matt Mackall proposed using get_random_bytes() (SHA-1 based) for stronger randomness, but Linus objected due to performance — SHA-1 variant is ~2x slower (.660us vs .326us per call) and would cost ~1% of fork() performance. Linus's counter-proposal: keep hash state between invocations of half_md4_transform(), mixing in pid+jiffies, which strengthens the existing MD4-based RNG without major performance impact. Mackall preferred SHA-1 but accepted the compromise; Ingo Molnar added a small tweak. Commenters pointed to OpenBSD's arc4random() as better practice (faster and cryptographically stronger). PaX team clarified ASLR was never meant to protect against local attacks — only remote ones, since local bruteforce is trivial.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/332974/][Seccomp and sandboxing]]: Google's Chrome browser wants seccomp (PR_SET_SECCOMP) for Linux sandboxing — original mode 1 only allows read/write/exit/sigreturn. Google developers propose "mode 2" allowing a bitmask of permitted system calls. Ingo Molnar suggests using ftrace's event filtering instead — can filter syscalls and their arguments with arbitrary boolean expressions (e.g. "fd == 0" for read). James Morris criticizes lack of threat model and "yet another security scheme being designed on the fly." Seccomp mode 2 seen as simpler and more likely to merge than ftrace-based sandbox.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/333755/][EGLIBC: Not a fork, but a glibc distribution]]: EGLIBC (Embedded GLIBC) — CodeSourcery-sponsored drop-in glibc replacement focused on embedded configurability via option groups (disable IPv6, NIS, RPC, internationalization, etc. to reduce size). Debian announced switching to EGLIBC; aims for full source/binary compatibility with glibc. Arises partly from difficulty working with Ulrich Drepper's blunt maintainership style. Requires copyright assignment to FSF like glibc; changes merged back to glibc frequently. Debate over strlcpy/strlcat inclusion — rejected for compatibility, noted that OpenBSD and Solaris versions have differing behavior.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/333825/][Enabling DRM in the kernel?]]: Intel TXT (Trusted Execution Technology) patches resubmitted for Linux — enables "trusted boot" verifying kernel integrity before execution. Ted Ts'o warns TXT's primary purpose is enabling uncrackable DRM (not just trusted computing benefits like voting machines/medical devices). Unlike old TCPA, TXT allows locked environments that survive kernel updates without "Circuit City" re-authorization. James Morris notes Linus's stance ("DRM is perfectly ok with Linux!") and that kernel hackers shouldn't decide social policy. Limited community resistance; TXT likely to be merged eventually. DRM debate recaps GPLv2 vs GPLv3 tension over anti-circumvention.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/334747/][Sanitizing kernel memory]]: Larry Highsmith's patch series to clear ("sanitize") kernel pages on free to prevent information leaks — adapted from PaX. Initial approach used page flags to mark sensitive data (crypto, audit, key handling), but Alan Cox and Ingo Molnar objected: page flags are scarce, and marking what's sensitive actually helps attackers. Final approach: boot param "sanitize_mem" clears *all* freed kernel memory; also patches to kfree() and kmem_cache_free() clear sub-page allocations. Prevents leaks from kernel bugs but not cold boot physical attacks. Aimed at 2.6.31.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/334649/][Compcache: in-memory compressed swapping]]: Nitin Gupta's compcache/ramzswap project — creates compressed ram-based swap device. Pages swapped to ramzswap are compressed and stored in memory itself, avoiding disk I/O. Uses LZO compression and custom xvmalloc allocator (O(1), low fragmentation, supports highmem) — SLOB and SLUB unsuitable for tight-memory use. Benchmark: 168ms/355ms disk swap latencies reduced to 12µs/7µs. Used in Ubuntu netbook editions, Edubuntu installer, LTSP. In-kernel module series targeting 2.6.32; lacks worst-case performance data and hibernate awareness. Future: compress xvmalloc pages to disk, adaptive cache resizing.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/334546/][An updated guide to debugfs]]: Jonathan Corbet updates his 2004 debugfs article to cover 2.6.30 API. debugfs is a no-rules filesystem for kernel-to-userspace info export (unlike /proc/process-info or sysfs's one-value-per-file). API: debugfs_create_dir(), debugfs_create_file() with custom fops, plus helpers for u8/u16/u32/u64 (decimal and hex variants), bool, size_t, blob_wrapper, symlink, rename. Critical: no auto-cleanup on module unload — must call debugfs_remove() or debugfs_remove_recursive() explicitly. Mounted at /sys/kernel/debug, GPL-only export.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/335768/][How many page flags do we really have?]]: Investigation into struct page flags shortage — 24 flags defined on 32-bit, leaving ~8 free, but NUMA node and sparse memory "section number" bits occupy top of flags field, so practically zero free flags on 32-bit NUMA systems. Andi Kleen's HWPOISON patches hit this limit; proposals to use reserved bit or combine existing flags were rejected. Andi argues the problem is overstated: 64-bit has plenty of room, 32-bit NUMA node count is small and shrinking, and section number can be moved to separate array. Andrew Morton wants him to prove the latter. Relates to earlier sanitize_mem patch which also consumed a page flag.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/335415/][Okular, Debian, and copy restrictions]]: Okular PDF reader enforces PDF "no copy" flag by default — shows "copy forbidden by DRM" dialog when selecting text from restricted PDFs (Settings > configure Okular > Obey DRM checkbox, enabled by default). John Goerzen filed Debian bug; KDE maintainers refused to change default, citing PDF spec compliance and corporate legal needs (e.g. medical device SOPs). Free software advocates argue defaults should serve users, not restrictions; Evince and Xpdf patched this out years ago. Options: disable DRM flag (4 clicks), per-document config via ~/.kde/share/config/okularpartrc: ObeyDRM=false, or use Evince instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/335812/][Xen again]]: Status of Xen Dom0 merge efforts — failed to make 2.6.30 or likely 2.6.31. Jeremy Fitzhardinge argues Xen runs 500k+ servers including Amazon. Core objections: (1) Xen uses user-space hypervisor with set-in-stone ABI that Linux must maintain forever — Steven Rostedt and Thomas Gleixner oppose; propose merging Xen hypervisor into kernel tree to make it an internal interface instead. (2) Dom0 patches pollute x86 core code with too many "if (xen)..." conditionals — Linus calls Xen "a total mess from a development standpoint." (3) paravirt_ops layer imposes ~1% overhead on ALL kernels (even non-Xen) — Nick Piggin disabled it in default SLES11 kernel. Debate over whether to merge-then-fix vs fix-then-merge. KVM noted as cleaner alternative but requires hardware VT support; Xen PV still superior for performance on older CPUs without VT.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/335698/][Page sanitization, part 2]]: Follow-up to sanitize_mem patch controversy. Larry Highsmith's condescending responses alienated developers: called critics part of a "cabal of vagueness," dismissed kzfree() suggestion from SLAB maintainer Pekka Enberg as "hopeless" without evidence. Technical disputes: Peter Zijlstra concerned about cache eviction from zero-on-free, vs Arjan van de Ven arguing zero-on-allocate also has costs; Alan Cox says security outweighs performance for those who need it. Highsmith also posted kzfree() patches for tty buffers, 802.11 keys, crypto API — Linus called them unnecessary (plain memset() sufficient) and Herbert Xu noted crypto API patch zeros metadata gratuitously. Highsmith threatened to refer to these responses when future kernel vulnerabilities appear. Jake Edge editorializes this as a lesson in how NOT to get patches merged. Feature still not merged.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/336224/][Linux kernel design patterns - part 1]]: Neil Brown's three-part series on kernel design patterns. Part 1 covers reference counting: kref (atomic_dec_and_test, object freed when last ref dropped, e.g. s_active in super_block), kcref/"cached" (atomic_dec_and_lock, object can persist after last external ref, e.g. i_count in inode, d_count in dentry), plain (just atomic_dec when lifetime tied to parent, e.g. b_count in buffer_head). Also covers anti-pattern of using "bias" in refcounts to store state bits (e.g. S_BIAS in superblock, sysfs s_active negative bias) — should use flag bits instead.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/336255/][Linux kernel design patterns - part 2]]: Part 2 covers complex data structures: Embedded Anchor pattern (embed struct list_head/struct rb_node in objects, use container_of to find parent — enables multiple lists per object); Broad Interfaces (20 list_for_each macros vs one-size-fits-all, 6 radix_tree lookup variants — right tool for each job); Tool Box (provide building blocks not complete solutions — rbtree has no search function, caller writes one with their comparator to avoid function-pointer overhead); Caller Locks (leave locking to callers); Preallocate Outside Locks (radix_tree_preload fills per-CPU pool before taking lock).
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/338435/][Protected RAMFS]]: Marco Stornelli's PRAMFS for embedded NVRAM devices — write-protected RAM filesystem marking PTEs read-only, writing only with locks held. Forces O_DIRECT to avoid page cache, supports execute-in-place (XIP). No journal, uses checksums for superblock/inode integrity. Mount with physaddr=0x20000000,init=size. Rejected for mainline: lacks hard links, no recovery/journaling, low performance (13Mbps char writes, 35Mbps block — Pavel Machek: "pretty low for RAM"), excessive locking. Called "niche filesystem" not "fully-featured."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/336262/][Linux kernel design patterns - part 3]]: Part 3 covers subsystem design — the "Midlayer Mistake" pattern: midlayers (imposed uniform code) should be replaced with libraries (optional building blocks). Examples: Block layer moved elevator to optional via blk_queue_make_request() (library approach), VFS page_cache is a good library (8 of 13 ext3 file_ops are generic), but dcache is a midlayer imposing loop-detection on all filesystems. MD/RAID layer is a messy mix of midlayer and library features. Key identifiers of midlayer: special cases, imposed functionality, struct containing pointers to subordinate structs, unrelated code grouped together. subsystems like cfg80211/mac80211 (wireless) are sometimes a necessary midlayer.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/338981/][VFAT patent avoidance and patent workarounds]]: Jonathan Corbet covers IBM's new VFAT patent workaround patch (by Andrew Tridgell), following the rejected "no long file names" patch from May. The key insight: Microsoft's patents claim creating *both* long and short names for the same file, not long names alone. The patch avoids this by creating only a long-name entry while filling the required FAT short-name slot with a deliberately invalid entry (starts with space+NUL, ends with slash extension) that cannot be listed, opened, or considered a name. Comes with a legal FAQ (IBM reviewed). The article defends workarounds as cost-effective alternatives to patent invalidation (years, millions of dollars, uncertain outcome). Critical point from the FAQ: publicly questioning a workaround's effectiveness can undermine it in court by suggesting willful infringement and defeating summary judgment — concerns should go to private communication. Corbet argues the community must get good at this game since it cannot opt out.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/339314/][Stallman warns about C# and Mono dependence]]: Jake Edge covers RMS's warning that relying on Mono or C# applications is a "serious danger" due to Microsoft software patent risk — triggered by Debian adding Mono-based Tomboy to a GNOME meta-package. Stallman's nuance: implementing C# (GNU Portable.NET exists) is fine; the danger is in *applications* written in C# that we would lose if patents are enforced. His prescription: discourage new C# development, prefer Mono-free alternatives (e.g. Gnote over Tomboy in C++), and exclude Mono from default installs. Fedora 12 dropped Tomboy for Gnote (primarily for footprint, not patents). Red Hat ships no Mono in RHEL. Open Invention Network added Mono to its protected list, giving distributions like Fedora enough cover to ship it. Ubuntu Technical Board sees no reason to exclude Mono. Jo Shields's defense: Mono is driven by applications (F-Spot, GNOME Do, Banshee), the anti-Mono crowd are "back-seat drivers," and OIN coverage reduces patent risk. Article concludes the real problem is software patents broadly, and Microsoft's silence on intentions is itself a tactic.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/339337/][Soft updates, hard problems]]: Valerie Aurora explains why soft updates — the BSD FFS crash-consistency technique — is rarely implemented elsewhere despite impressive benchmarks (within 5% of in-memory FS performance vs. 20–30% slower for synchronous FFS). Soft updates avoids journaling by: (1) carefully ordering writes so only "allocated-but-free" inconsistencies can occur at crash time, then (2) running fsck in the background on a file system snapshot after reboot to reclaim leaked resources. The three ordering rules: never point to a structure before it's initialized; never re-use a resource before nullifying all pointers to it; never reset an old pointer before the new one is set. Cyclical dependencies (block A must precede B and B must precede A) are broken by lock/roll-back/write/roll-forward on each block write. The implementation requires 12 distinct dependency-tracking structures (bmsafemap, inodedep, allocdirect, indirdep, allocindir, pagedep, mkdir, dirrem, freefrag, freeblks, freefile) each manually designed per file system operation. The fatal flaw: every new FS feature or format change requires deriving all new ordering rules and custom structures from scratch — there is no general transaction abstraction. This tight coupling causes user-visible bugs (rmdir's ".." entry can delay parent link-count decrement by up to 2 minutes, breaking "rm -rf"). Journaling and copy-on-write provide a simpler open/add-block/close transaction interface requiring no special knowledge. Aurora concludes soft updates is "an evolutionary dead end."
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/339349/][VA API slowly -- but surely -- making progress]]: Nathan Willis surveys the Video Acceleration API (VA API), launched December 2006 by Intel's Jonathan Bian as a modern replacement for XvMC, which only supported MPEG-2 motion compensation. VA API adds VC-1, MPEG-4 ASP/H.263, MPEG-4 AVC/H.264, iDCT, VLD (slice-level acceleration), deblocking, IZZ, color space conversion, scaling, and (since spec 0.30, March 2009) hardware video *encoding* (H.263, H.264, MPEG-4). Only Intel's closed-source psb-video driver for the Poulsbo/GMA500 chipset natively implements VA API; NVIDIA uses VDPAU and ATI uses XvBA, both proprietary and non-encoding. Gwenole Beauchesne (Splitted-Desktop Systems) wrote open-source bridge libraries (vdpau-video, xvba-video) allowing VA API calls to dispatch to VDPAU or XvBA backends, plus patched libVA and patched MPlayer/FFmpeg. VLC added VA API in its development branch. CPU utilization results: 66–98% unaccelerated vs. as low as 0.6% with VA API acceleration. S3 Graphics added a VA API driver for Chrome 400/500 in Feb 2009. Open source Nouveau and RadeonHD drivers are not pursuing hardware video acceleration. Future plans include G45 chipset support and rendering directly to OpenGL textures (for Clutter integration). The article notes VA API is the only vendor-neutral open hardware acceleration standard, but adoption remains limited by closed-driver dependency.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/340080/][Transcendent memory]]: Jonathan Corbet covers Dan Magenheimer's "transcendent memory" concept — a class of RAM of unknown and dynamically variable size, addressable only indirectly, configurable as persistent or ephemeral (may disappear without warning), and synchronously accessible. Implemented as page pools: tmem_new_pool() creates a pool identified by 128-bit UUID with flags for ephemeral bit, shared bit, page order, and version; tmem_put_page() / tmem_get_page() copy pages in/out by (pool_id, object_id, page_id) key tuple. Either call may fail — callers must have a fallback. Ephemeral tmem_get_page() may fail even after a successful put (hypervisor reclaimed the page). Two use cases: (1) precache — page cache stores about-to-be-evicted clean pages into an ephemeral pool, retrieving them later to avoid disk I/O; (2) preswap — swap writes to persistent pool instead of disk, saving I/O at both swap-out and swap-in. The hypervisor manages pool sizing, can coalesce duplicate pages (KSM-style), and can prioritize pools by guest activity. Key security concern: shared pools identified only by UUID allow a compromised guest to access another guest's data if it obtains the UUID; Dan dropped shared pool support in v2 pending a proper security model. Needs performance benchmarks before mainline inclusion.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/340400/][A lockless ring-buffer]]: Jake Edge covers Steven Rostedt's proposed lockless ring-buffer algorithm for the kernel's common tracing infrastructure (targeting 2.6.32), replacing the lock-heavy implementation merged in 2.6.28. The buffer is a circular doubly-linked list of pages with head, tail, and commit pointers — tail marks where new writes go, commit marks the latest completed write (needed because interrupt-nested writers can interleave). A separate reader page is detached from the list to prevent writer corruption. The lockless trick: pointers are 4-byte aligned, freeing 2 low bits for flags — HEADER (points to current head page) and UPDATE (page is volatile, being claimed by a writer). Readers swap the reader page for the head page using cmpxchg() requiring the HEADER flag; writers transitioning to a new tail page check for HEADER and set it to UPDATE, causing any concurrent reader cmpxchg() to fail and retry. Two modes: overwrite ("flight recorder") where new events overwrite old, and producer/consumer where writes to a full buffer fail. Edge case: if nested writes fill the buffer such that tail reaches the commit page on the reader page, writers must detect this and drop writes rather than push head forward (which would corrupt the commit pointer's path back into the ring). The algorithm depends on disabling preemption, ruling out direct use in user-space tracing. Mathieu Desnoyers (LTTng) notes complexity is "near that of RCU" and lacks formal proof; he plans an LTTng-based alternative. Subject to a Red Hat patent application by Rostedt, with a free-software use commitment.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/340495/][Tiny Core Linux 2.1: Less is more]]: Koen Vervloesem reviews Tiny Core Linux 2.1, an 11 MB ISO distribution by Robert Shingledecker (formerly of Damn Small Linux) targeting 486DX/48 MB RAM minimums, running kernel 2.6.29, BusyBox, Tiny X, FLTK, and JWM window manager entirely from RAM. Four operation modes: Cloud/Internet (default, fully RAM-based, non-persistent); PPR/TCE (persistent partition using tar.gz TCE extensions loaded into RAM at boot — can exhaust memory with many extensions); PPR/TCZ (persistent partition using cramfs/ziofs compressed mountable TCZ images, symlinked into rootfs via /tmp/tcloop — only uses RAM when app runs); PPI/TCE (extensions installed to a Linux partition/loopback file for faster boot and RAM savings). Extension install via "appbrowser" package manager with basic dependency resolution. Hundreds of packages available including Firefox 3.5, GIMP, MPlayer, Java. Persistent home via home=hdXY boot option; encrypted home via cryptohome=hdXY with a loopback-file AES volume. Remastering is straightforward (copy ISO contents, add/remove files, repack). Micro Core Linux variant is 7 MB, boots to BusyBox prompt with no X. Weaknesses: no out-of-box wireless, basic package manager, no simple hard-drive installer. Best use case: portable USB environment.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/341110/][Communicating requirements to kernel developers]]: Jonathan Corbet covers a 2009 kernel summit pre-discussion thread (started by Matthew Garrett after the Gran Canaria Desktop Summit) on why userspace developers struggle to get requirements into the kernel. User-side barriers: LKML volume is overwhelming; posts get buried; kernel jargon is opaque; patch-or-go-away culture; threat of flame responses. Developer-side failures: no feature-request tracking mechanism; developers are insular and busy; tone is harsh even if improved. Bad-request patterns identified: framing requests as demands (e.g., early CGL specs read as a to-do list); wrong abstraction level (early TALPA posted low-level file-close interception requirements instead of the high-level goal of virus scanning support, setting the project back significantly). James Bottomley's three categories: (1) developers who write their own extensions; (2) users who find an interested kernel developer; (3) users with no kernel access who remain stuck. Ted Ts'o's four categories: core developers (high trust, easy merge); competent non-core developers (must justify more); developers with "patently bad design taste" (filtered by frank review); users with no kernel access (subdivided by whether an active developer works in their area). Corbet notes Andrew Morton informally fills the community ombudsman role but cannot scale to it full-time; the community needs a dedicated person to clarify requirements, connect users with developers, and track requests — a position that is very hard to fund.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/341352/][A new way to truncate() files]]: Goldwyn Rodrigues covers Nick Piggin's patch series reworking the kernel's truncate() call sequence. The old sequence: do_sys_truncate() → do_truncate() → notify_change() → inode_setattr() → vmtruncate() (changes i_size first) → filesystem truncate() — problem: i_size is already changed before the filesystem is called, so the filesystem cannot fail the operation, doesn't know the previous i_size, and generic error-path block deallocation had no clean way to return errors. New sequence introduces: inode_newsize_ok(inode, offset) — centralises size limit and swap-file checks currently duplicated across filesystems; truncate_pagecache(inode, old, new) — unmaps pages beyond new size and should be called before filesystem releases disk blocks so writepage() is never called on deallocated blocks; simple_setsize() — replaces vmtruncate() in inode_setattr() when ATTR_SIZE is passed. Filesystems wanting the new behaviour set new_truncate=1 in inode_operations (admitted "nasty hack" for the transition period) and implement their own setsize() that validates size, truncates page cache, and frees disk blocks — clearing ATTR_SIZE before calling generic inode_setattr() to avoid double processing. vmtruncate() is deprecated. ext2 is the demonstration conversion (ext2_setsize()). The patchset is motivated by closing a race in page_mkwrite() where a truncate-beyond-current-size could leave PTEs pointing at uninitialized blocks.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/341244/][Is pre-linking worth it?]]: Jake Edge examines prelink, which pre-computes dynamic linker relocations and stores results in ELF sections to speed program startup — but Fedora Rawhide bugs have made its value questionable. The benefit: avoids most relocation cost at load time; pages that don't change for relocations stay shared via COW between processes (Ulrich Drepper's key motivation). Jakob Jelinek posted OpenOffice Writer numbers showing 10x relocation time difference. The cost: libraries at fixed addresses for 2 weeks weaken ASLR (address space layout randomization) — on 64-bit this is less concerning; binary integrity verification is harder (prelink modifies binaries); prelink must re-run after every library update; VDSO overlap on 32-bit forces some relocation anyway. Moblin (Arjan van de Ven) measured 15-20% disk read reduction during boot from avoiding page-fault loads of relocation data. Matthew Miller (Fedora) questioned whether fragility and forensic difficulty justify the gains for long-running servers. KDE's nix noted C++ libraries with thousands of long symbols (avg 31.9 chars) and hidden-visibility problems make relocation expensive even with GCC's .gnu.hash — prelink is "not small" for KDE-scale apps. The -Bdirect linking proposal (which would resolve symbols at compile time) was shot down by Drepper ("we have prelinking").
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/342330/][Fun with NULL pointers, part 1]]: Jonathan Corbet dissects Brad Spengler's 2.6.30 local kernel exploit. The chain: Herbert Xu's TUN driver patch added a sock pointer dereference before a NULL check in tun_chr_poll(); the NULL tun pointer was reachable because Herbert Xu's accounting patch set it to NULL on a specific ioctl; the zero page (normally unmapped to catch NULL derefs) was mappable because CONFIG_SECURITY modules (e.g. SELinux) override mmap_min_addr checks at compile time, and Red Hat's default SELinux policy allows mapping page zero; the exploit uses personality(SVR4) with a setuid pulseaudio to map the zero page; GCC optimizes away the NULL check since the pointer was already dereferenced; the writable NULL lets test_and_set_bit(SOCK_ASYNC_NOSPACE) flip a single bit in memory — pointing into the TUN file_operations mmap pointer (normally NULL) and setting it to 1; calling mmap() on the TUN fd jumps to address 1 in the zero page, which contains exploit shellcode, achieving arbitrary kernel code execution. Multiple failures required: security modules overriding mmap_min_addr; SELinux allowing zero-page maps; pulseaudio setuid-root with module-loading; GCC NULL-check elimination; TUN mmap pointer being flippable. taviso and Julien Tinnes had independently discovered the personality() mmap_min_addr bypass. Fix: move the dereference after the NULL check; disable page-zero mapping by default.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/342582/][Fighting small bugs]]: Bruce Byfield covers Ubuntu's "One Hundred Paper Cuts" and Fedora's "Fit and Finish" projects, both targeting low-severity usability bugs to improve desktop experience. Canonical's David Siegel launched One Hundred Paper Cuts for Karmic Koala (Oct 2009), focusing on GNOME panel and Nautilus "space between applications" bugs from a user-submitted pool; organized as 10 rounds of 11 bugs each; must be fixable by one developer in one day. Fedora's Matthias Clasen organized Fit and Finish as topic-based test days (display config, batteries, peripherals) with less formal structure and no fixed bug count. Early results: Paper Cuts achieved ~80% fix rate in first two rounds; Fit and Finish had lower initial turnout (clashes with Desktop Summit). Key tension: upstream GNOME doesn't share the same urgency for small bugs. Both projects encourage user participation and first-time bug reporters. Usability testing via collaborative bug isolation is cited as a significant side benefit.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/342629/][The grumpy editor's e-book reader]]: Jonathan Corbet reviews the Amazon Kindle 2 after receiving it as a birthday gift. The Kindle runs Linux (2.6.22.19), uses an e-ink display, has a 3G modem (whispernet), and shows up as USB mass storage — but notably lacks a PDF reader. Corbet uses calibre (GPLv3, Python) to convert PDFs to MOBI format for transfer; the conversion degrades formatting (vanishing paragraph breaks, absorbed headers/footers, lost code indentation). Savory runs as a daemon on the Kindle itself, converting PDFs to MOBI and also producing PNG page images — slow but functional. The "fetch news" feature in calibre downloads and converts newspapers automatically. On GPL compliance: Amazon publishes kernel source (140 MB tarball with 44 inner tarballs) for 2.6.22.19, GCC, bootchart, powertop, iptables, and patches for Freescale i.MX27/i.MX31 processors, yaffs2 filesystem, and various Kindle-specific hardware — but the source offer was hard to find and the code is not upstream. Corbet notes the Kindle is a "closed, captive platform"; the 1984 remote-deletion incident (where Amazon deleted purchased copies of Orwell from user devices) illustrates that running Linux doesn't mean serving the owner. Openinkpot (Hanlin v3/N516) and iLiad are cited as more open alternatives. Corbet observes no one has achieved root on Kindle 2.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/343608/][A new GCC runtime library license snag?]]: Jonathan Corbet covers a licensing problem Florian Weimer raised with GCC 4.4's runtime library (libgcc). GCC 4.4 moved to GPLv3, with an exception allowing proprietary use — but this creates incompatibility with GPLv2-only programs like git and udev. The issue: GPLv2's "system library" exception says linking a system library with a GPLv2 program doesn't create a derived work — but it contains an "unless that component itself accompanies the executable" clause. Since distributors ship GCC (the "component") alongside programs, the exception doesn't apply to the GCC runtime library, making distribution of GPLv2-only programs with libgcc technically a GPLv2 violation. The perverse result: GPLv2-only projects can't be distributed with GCC 4.4. The FSF's runtime exception tries to solve this but can't bind non-FSF copyright holders. Debian (which triggered the discovery) faces a dilemma. Potential fixes: FSF issue a statement blessing the combination (not binding on others); relicense libgcc to GPLv2+ (foom's suggestion); or await a fork. Corbet notes the FSF has been unresponsive to Weimer's queries since April 2009.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/343768/][GNOME on non-Linux platforms]]: Jake Edge covers a gnome-desktop-devel thread started by Christian Schaller proposing that GNOME officially acknowledge it's Linux-only, ending the pretense of multi-platform support for Solaris and BSD. Sun engineers pushed back, arguing for continued platform neutrality and better upstream involvement. The core tension: GNOME now fills in "complete operating system" functionality (USB, power management, networking) that POSIX+X11 alone don't cover, making cross-platform portability harder. David Zeuthen notes Solaris often gets GNOME features only after Linux implementations are set in stone — better upstream participation in design would catch portability issues earlier. Jason Clinton observes OpenSolaris has "cathedral-like" legacy design in subsystems making GNOME work "so, so much more" than on BSD. Colin Walters argues the problem can't be abstracted away: someone must write platform-specific code. Kachitchkine's proposal: N-way commitment from all platforms with upfront portability work, distributing QA burden and reducing iterations. BSD camp was notably silent. Commenters noted KDE's Qt-based approach makes portability easier; some suggested GNOME may need similar abstraction.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/343828/][A tempest in a tty pot]]: Jonathan Corbet describes the TTY layer as "one of the very few pieces of kernel code that scares the hell out of" Ingo Molnar — ancient code (copyright 1991,1992 Linus), still on the big kernel lock, with deadlocks and race conditions. Alan Cox had been successfully modernizing it, but 2.6.31 introduced regressions: kdesu broke (KDE bug: read too much data), emacs compile mode lost output (SIGCHLD from exiting subprocess arrived before data was pushed through the PTY line discipline). The line discipline processes data asynchronously, so close() can return before discipline runs — standards-compliant but breaks emacs. Alan posted a fix but Linus was furious Alan blamed emacs, demanding to know why kernel regressions were dismissed as user-land bugs. Alan responded with frustration, posted a patch removing himself as TTY maintainer, and walked away. The TTY layer is now unmaintained again with known regressions in 2.6.31. Thread spawned a sidebar on kernel API stability vs BSD-style integrated development; mikov argued Linux's model forces vendors to maintain out-of-tree drivers and makes long-term support impossible.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/344117/][A desktop "secrets" API]]: Jake Edge covers a freedesktop.org initiative by Michael Leupold (KDE Wallet) and Stef Walter (GNOME Keyring) to create a unified Secrets API across desktop environments. Users run Keyring or Wallet as a login-session daemon; applications use the D-Bus-based API without caring which backend provides it. Secrets are opaque byte arrays with lookup attributes (key-value pairs), labels, properties, and can be grouped into collections. Items/collections can be locked, requiring user password to unlock. The API allows optional encryption in transit, but docs explicitly warn it won't withstand man-in-the-middle or active attacks — intended to prevent plaintext in swap/memory caches, not to secure the D-Bus session. Java/C# /Python apps can't prevent their own memory paging, making encrypted transit of limited value for them. Higher-level topics under discussion: browser password identification for cross-browser migration, secret never leaving the daemon (for smartcards), and "smart user accounts" where a privileged daemon acts on behalf of the user without revealing secrets. Mozilla hasn't engaged; Firefox/Thunderbird integration lags. Plan 9's secstore/factotum cited as a more sophisticated prior art.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/347006/][Another kernel NULL pointer vulnerability]]: Tavis Ormandy discovered sock_sendpage() dereferences an unvalidated function pointer in proto_ops structures for PF_APPLETALK, PF_IPX, PF_IRDA, PF_X25, PF_AX25, PF_BLUETOOTH, PF_IUCV, PF_INET6/SSCTP, PF_PPPOX, PF_ISDN. Local privilege escalation via page-zero mapping (exploits mmap_min_addr bypass via SELinux policy on RHEL). Fix committed by Linus Aug 13, 2009; mmap_min_addr > 0 mitigates.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/347511/][The trouble with discard]]: Jonathan Corbet examines discard/TRIM for SSDs — ATA TRIM is non-queued, causing I/O stalls; actual SSD delays measure hundreds of milliseconds. Matthew Wilcox proposes block-layer discard caching; Christoph Hellwig implemented batched discard in XFS. Corbet argues SSDs are computers running proprietary firmware we'd be better off without; open flash access (UBI) could solve the problem correctly instead of working around vendor translation layers.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/347619/][A new API for kfifo?]]: Stefani Seibold proposed redesigned kfifo (23 functions vs 9) with Neil Brown design patterns — spinlock no longer required by default, supports embedding in structs, user-space copy variants, fixed-length records. Alan Cox/Andrew Morton disagreement on whether kfifo_in() should assume locking (Morton won); unlikely in 2.6.31, possible for 2.6.32.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/348886/][HWPOISON]]: Jon Ashburn covers Andi Kleen/Fengguang Wu's memory poisoning for hardware with MCA Recovery (Intel Nehalem-EX). Uncorrectable ECC errors mark pages poisoned rather than panic; handler isolates corrupted pages, finds affected processes via reverse mapping, kills with SIGBUS (vm.memory_failure_early_kill) or delays until corrupted data is accessed. Supports clean page cache (drop and re-read from disk), dirty page cache (invalidate + I/O error on next access), swap cache (delay kill until reference). User-mode fault injector via debugfs for testing.
- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/347573/][Runtime power management]]: Jonathan Corbet covers Rafael Wysocki's runtime PM infrastructure for 2.6.32 — dev_pm_ops with runtime_suspend/resume/idle callbacks; drivers prepare devices for low-power states without full system suspend. Async suspend/resume patch enables parallel device suspend/resume using completion objects to track dependencies. Matthew Garrett pushed runtime PM into PCI/USB bus layers, saving ~0.2W. Problem: device tree can't represent all PM dependencies; pm_link structure added to track master/slave device relationships.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/348469/][Walsh: Secure Virtualization Using SELinux (sVirt)]]: Jake Edge covers Dan Walsh's sVirt — using SELinux to isolate virtual machines. Problem: if hypervisor is compromised, attacker owns all VMs and can write to host disk images. sVirt applies security labels to VMs, images, and devices via libvirt; each guest gets isolated MCS (multi-category security) range. Automatic labeling: no need for admin to manually label disk locations. Fedora 12 extends to USB/PCI device assignment. - [[https://lwn.net/Articles/348719/][Page-based direct I/O]]: Jonathan Corbet covers Jens Axboe's redesigned direct I/O interface for 2.6.32+. Old direct_IO() took user-space iovec buffers; new dio_args structure groups parameters including pages array, offset, length. VFS now handles user-to-page address translation. Fixes loopback driver double-caching (file mounted via loopback wastes page cache). Eliminates potential performance problem from processing one I/O segment at a time. Andrew Morton objected to hidden head_page index in struct dio — "disaster waiting to happen." Database workloads (Oracle developer) are biggest consumers of direct I/O.

- [[https://lwn.net/Articles/350390/][What the Internet knows about you]]: Brief note on whattheinternetknowsaboutyou.com — CSS history disclosure vulnerability allowing sites to detect visited links without JavaScript.
